Proof for the Attribute of al-Yad (Hand)
Al-Shaykh's proof for the attribute of yad with Quranic verse and argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shaykh's proof for the divine attribute of hand (yad) from the Quranic verse and his argument against interpreting it as mere power.

Core Idea
Allah's hands are a real attribute, not a metaphor for power.

This lesson introduces the first of three attribute proofs. Al-Shaykh cites the verse where Allah addresses Iblis and mentions 'My two hands.' He argues that interpreting 'hands' as power would make the specification meaningless, since all creation is by power. This establishes that the attribute 'yad' is real and befitting Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the proof of yad. Notice the verse and the logical argument that follows.
The Arabic is short but precise.
2
Understand the verse
The verse is from Surah Sad, where Allah asks Iblis why he didn't prostrate to what He created with His two hands.
3
Analyze the argument
Al-Shaykh argues that if 'two hands' meant power, the specification would be pointless because all creation is by power. Therefore, 'yad' must be a distinct attribute.
This is an argument from the uselessness of specification (takhsis).
4
Consider the implication
This proof shows that the attribute 'yad' is affirmed as a real attribute, not metaphorical. It is befitting Allah without resemblance to created hands.
This is a standard Ash'ari approach to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليله على الصفة التي تُسمَّى بـ ( اليد ) : قوله تعالى لإبليس : مَا مَنَعَكَ أَن تَسْجُدَ لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَى [ص : ٧٥] قالَ : ولا وجه لحمل اليدين على القدرةِ ؛ إذْ جملة المبتدَعاتِ مخترعةٌ بالقدرة ، ففي الحمل على ذلك إبطال فائدة التخصيص ودليله على إثباتِ الصفةِ التي تُسمى بـ ( العينِ ) : قوله تعالى : وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي ﴾ [طه : ٣٩] . ودليله على الصفةِ التي تُسمَّى بـ ( الوجه ) : قوله تعالى : وَيَبْقَى وَجْهُ رَبِّكَ ذُو الْجَلَالِ وَالْإِكْرَامِ (۱) [الرحمن : ٢٧]
And his proof for the attribute called 'al-yad' (the hand) is His statement, the Most High, to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating to what I created with My two hands?' [Sad: 75]. He said: There is no justification for carrying the two hands upon power, since the sum total of originated things are created by power. So in carrying it upon that there is nullification of the benefit of specification. And his proof for affirming the attribute called 'al-'ayn' (the eye) is His statement, the Most High: 'And that you might be formed under My eye' [Taha: 39]. And his proof for the attribute called 'al-wajh' (the face) is His statement, the Most High: 'And there will remain the Face of your Lord, the Owner of Majesty and Honor' [al-Rahman: 27].
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents three short proofs for the divine attributes of yad, 'ayn, and wajh. For each, al-Shaykh cites a Quranic verse and argues that the apparent meaning must be upheld rather than interpreted metaphorically. For the hand (yad), he uses the verse in which Allah says He created Adam with His two hands. The argument is that if 'hands' were interpreted as power, the specification would be pointless because all creation is by power. The same logic applies to 'ayn (eye) and wajh (face). The passage does not elaborate further but implies that these attributes are real and befitting Allah.

Key Terms
اليد
القدرة
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attribute 'yad' is metaphorical and means power.
Correction: The passage argues that if it meant power, the specification would be pointless, so it must be a real attribute.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Shaykh reject interpreting 'yaday' as power?
A) Because power is not mentioned in the verse.
B) Because all creation is by power, so the specification would be pointless.
C) Because Iblis was addressed.
D) Because the verse is about Adam specifically.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of al-yad is affirmed as a real divine attribute, not a metaphor for power, based on the Quranic verse and the logic of specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah created Adam with His two hands, does your heart immediately accept it as true, or does your mind try to rationalize it away because it feels uncomfortable? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's words over your own reasoning?
?
The proof shows that if 'yad' only meant power, the specification would be meaningless. In your life, are there specific commands, supplications, or descriptions of Allah that you have reduced to 'just general concepts'? Will you commit today to honoring the specific words of Allah and His Messenger without excusing them away?
?
Allah honored Adam by creating him directly with His two hands. As a descendant of Adam, how does this realization affect your sense of dignity and responsibility before Allah? Does it make you more grateful? More humble? What one change will you make today to live in light of that honor?

Proof for the Attribute of al-'Ayn (Eye)
Al-Shaykh's proof for the attribute of 'ayn with Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shaykh's proof for the divine attribute of eye ('ayn) from the Quranic verse in Surat Taha.

Core Idea
Allah's eye is a real attribute, indicated by the verse about Prophet Musa.

This lesson focuses on the second attribute proof: al-'ayn (the eye). Al-Shaykh cites a verse where Allah says to Musa 'you were formed under My eye.' This indicates a real attribute of sight or supervision that is specific, not a general power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the brief passage for the proof of 'ayn. The verse is short but significant.
2
Understand the verse
The verse from Surat Taha speaks about Musa being formed under Allah's 'ayn' (eye). This implies special care and attention.
The context is about Musa's early life.
3
Apply the logic
Similar to the hand argument, if 'ayn' were just knowledge or power, the specification would be unnecessary. So it affirms a real attribute.
This follows the same pattern as the previous proof.
4
Consider the theological point
The attribute 'ayn' is affirmed without anthropomorphism, befitting Allah's majesty.
Ash'ari theology upholds these attributes as real but not like creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليله على الصفة التي تُسمَّى بـ ( اليد ) : قوله تعالى لإبليس : مَا مَنَعَكَ أَن تَسْجُدَ لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَى [ص : ٧٥] قالَ : ولا وجه لحمل اليدين على القدرةِ ؛ إذْ جملة المبتدَعاتِ مخترعةٌ بالقدرة ، ففي الحمل على ذلك إبطال فائدة التخصيص ودليله على إثباتِ الصفةِ التي تُسمى بـ ( العينِ ) : قوله تعالى : وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي ﴾ [طه : ٣٩] . ودليله على الصفةِ التي تُسمَّى بـ ( الوجه ) : قوله تعالى : وَيَبْقَى وَجْهُ رَبِّكَ ذُو الْجَلَالِ وَالْإِكْرَامِ (۱) [الرحمن : ٢٧]
And his proof for the attribute called 'al-yad' (the hand) is His statement, the Most High, to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating to what I created with My two hands?' [Sad: 75]. He said: There is no justification for carrying the two hands upon power, since the sum total of originated things are created by power. So in carrying it upon that there is nullification of the benefit of specification. And his proof for affirming the attribute called 'al-'ayn' (the eye) is His statement, the Most High: 'And that you might be formed under My eye' [Taha: 39]. And his proof for the attribute called 'al-wajh' (the face) is His statement, the Most High: 'And there will remain the Face of your Lord, the Owner of Majesty and Honor' [al-Rahman: 27].
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents three short proofs for the divine attributes of yad, 'ayn, and wajh. For each, al-Shaykh cites a Quranic verse and argues that the apparent meaning must be upheld rather than interpreted metaphorically. For the hand (yad), he uses the verse in which Allah says He created Adam with His two hands. The argument is that if 'hands' were interpreted as power, the specification would be pointless because all creation is by power. The same logic applies to 'ayn (eye) and wajh (face). The passage does not elaborate further but implies that these attributes are real and befitting Allah.

Key Terms
العين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attribute 'ayn' means Allah has a physical eye.
Correction: The passage affirms a real attribute, but it is not physical; it is befitting Allah's essence.

Checkpoint
Which verse is used as proof for the attribute of al-'ayn?
A) Sad: 75
B) Taha: 39
C) al-Rahman: 27
D) al-Ikhlas: 1

Key Takeaway
The attribute of al-'ayn is affirmed as a real divine attribute based on the Quranic verse showing special divine attention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt truly unseen by anyone, even yourself? The Qur'an tells you that you were formed under Allah's eye. Does that reality change how you live when no one is watching? What would you have to change if you took it seriously?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's eye is upon you at every moment—real, not just a metaphor—what is one thing you would do differently today, starting now?
?
The attribute of al-'ayn is not a distant theological concept; it means you are personally under His gaze. Are you living as if that is true? What would it mean to live with that awareness every second of your life?

Proof for the Attribute of al-Wajh (Face)
Al-Shaykh's proof for the attribute of wajh with Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shaykh's proof for the divine attribute of face (wajh) from the Quranic verse in Surat al-Rahman.

Core Idea
Allah's face is an eternal attribute, distinct from His essence.

This final lesson covers the proof for al-wajh (the face). The verse from al-Rahman states that only the Face of Allah will remain, implying it is a real attribute that does not perish. This serves as evidence that 'wajh' is an eternal attribute of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage for the proof of wajh. It is a single verse.
2
Understand the verse
The verse says everything will perish except Allah's Face. This indicates that 'Face' is something that remains, so it cannot be a metaphor for essence or existence.
If 'wajh' meant essence, the statement would be tautological.
3
Grasp the argument
The verse distinguishes between what perishes and what remains. The Face is mentioned as remaining, so it must be an attribute that is distinct from the essence in some way, yet eternal.
This supports the Ash'ari position that attributes like wajh are eternal.
4
Apply to theology
Thus, al-wajh is affirmed as a real, eternal attribute of Allah, not a body part, but a meaning befitting His majesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليله على الصفة التي تُسمَّى بـ ( اليد ) : قوله تعالى لإبليس : مَا مَنَعَكَ أَن تَسْجُدَ لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَى [ص : ٧٥] قالَ : ولا وجه لحمل اليدين على القدرةِ ؛ إذْ جملة المبتدَعاتِ مخترعةٌ بالقدرة ، ففي الحمل على ذلك إبطال فائدة التخصيص ودليله على إثباتِ الصفةِ التي تُسمى بـ ( العينِ ) : قوله تعالى : وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي ﴾ [طه : ٣٩] . ودليله على الصفةِ التي تُسمَّى بـ ( الوجه ) : قوله تعالى : وَيَبْقَى وَجْهُ رَبِّكَ ذُو الْجَلَالِ وَالْإِكْرَامِ (۱) [الرحمن : ٢٧]
And his proof for the attribute called 'al-yad' (the hand) is His statement, the Most High, to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating to what I created with My two hands?' [Sad: 75]. He said: There is no justification for carrying the two hands upon power, since the sum total of originated things are created by power. So in carrying it upon that there is nullification of the benefit of specification. And his proof for affirming the attribute called 'al-'ayn' (the eye) is His statement, the Most High: 'And that you might be formed under My eye' [Taha: 39]. And his proof for the attribute called 'al-wajh' (the face) is His statement, the Most High: 'And there will remain the Face of your Lord, the Owner of Majesty and Honor' [al-Rahman: 27].

Commentary

For the hand (yad), he uses the verse in which Allah says He created Adam with His two hands. The argument is that if 'hands' were interpreted as power, the specification would be pointless because all creation is by power. The same logic applies to 'ayn (eye) and wajh (face). The passage does not elaborate further but implies that these attributes are real and befitting Allah.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attribute 'wajh' means Allah has a physical face.
Correction: It is a real attribute, but not physical; it is a meaning that remains after everything else perishes.

Checkpoint
What does the verse 'There will remain the Face of your Lord' imply about the attribute of al-wajh?
A) It is a physical face.
B) It is eternal and distinct from the essence.
C) It is the same as Allah's essence.
D) It will perish.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of al-wajh is affirmed as an eternal, real attribute of Allah based on the Quranic verse stating it alone remains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The verse tells you that only the Face of your Lord remains. Everything you love, every person, every possession will perish. Are you living as if that is true, or have you attached your heart to what will not last? What one attachment do you need to loosen this week?
?
The proof shows that Allah's Face is a distinct attribute of majesty and honor. When you pray, do you truly feel you are standing before the One whose Face is majestic, or do you let your mind drift to worldly concerns? This week, before each prayer, pause and say, 'I am facing the Possessor of Majesty and Honor.' Will you do it?
?
You have learned that the specification of 'Face' is not redundant—it reveals a real attribute. How often do you rush past Allah's names and attributes without reflecting on their distinct meanings? Commit this week to meditate on one attribute (like al-Wajh) and let it transform how you speak or act.

Imam al-Haramayn's Ta'wīl of al-Istiwā' as Domination
Sanusi explains Imam al-Haramayn's ta'wīl of istiwā' as domination and the wisdom behind mentioning the Throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Haramayn's interpretation of istiwā' as domination (istīlā') and why the Throne is specifically mentioned in the verse.

Core Idea
Interpreting istiwā' as domination affirms Allah's absolute control and refutes any notion of independence in creation.

Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) offers a well-known ta'wīl of the verse of istiwā', interpreting it as Allah's complete domination over the Throne by subjugation and management. This reading preserves divine transcendence while addressing potential misconceptions about the Throne's independence. The passage below explains this interpretation and the wisdom behind the specific mention of the Throne.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the ta'wīl used by Imam al-Haramayn
Imam al-Haramayn interprets 'istawā' as 'istīlā' (domination) through subjugation (qahr) and management (tadbīr). Notice how he defines this.
3
Understand the reason for mentioning the Throne
The passage explains that even though all creation is equal in need of Allah, the Throne is specifically mentioned because its greatness might lead to a false sense of independence.
4
Follow the argument a fortiori
If the greatest creation (the Throne) is completely dependent on Allah, then all lesser creations are even more so.
5
Note the concluding remark
The passage ends by noting that there are other well-known interpretations of the verse, indicating that this is one among many acceptable ta'wīls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إمام الحرمينِ : فأَوَّلَ ( الاستواء ) في قوله تعالى : ﴿ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى بالاستيلاء عليه بالقهر والتدبير ؛ بحيثُ لا يتحرَّكُ ولا يسكنُ ، ولا يختص بالحيّز المعيَّن الذي يختص به ، ولا يتّصفُ بصفة عموماً .. إلا بإرادة مولانا جل وعزَّ ، وخَلْقِ ذلكَ فيهِ . ووجه تخصيصه بالذُّكرِ - وإنْ كانَتِ العوالم كلُّها تساويه فيما ذُكِرَ مِنْ عظيم الاحتياج إلى الباري تعالى ، وعدم استغنائها عنه لحظة - : أَنَّهُ لمَّا كانَ هو أعظم المخلوقاتِ ، ونسبةُ جميعها إليهِ كَحَلْقَةٍ مُلقاة في فلاةٍ مِنَ الأرض ، وربَّما تُوهَمَ أنَّ لهُ مِنَ القوَّةِ والرفعة ما يستغني بهِ في تدبير نفسه .. فنيَّة على أنَّهُ ـ على ما هو عليهِ مِنْ عظيم القوَّةِ وجلائل الصفات - مقهور محتاج غاية الاحتياج إلى مولانا جل وعزَّ ، لا يملك لنفسِهِ ولا لغيرِهِ ضرّاً ولا نفعاً ، ولا يُدبَّرُ أَمرَهُ حَمَلَتْهُ ولا أحدٌ مِنَ الخلق سواه تعالى ، وإذا ثبت في حقِّهِ ذلك ثبت في حقٌّ غيره أحرى ، وفي الآية تأويلات أُخرُ مشهورةٌ .
As for Imam al-Haramayn, he interpreted al-istiwā' in His statement, the Most High: {The Most Gracious rose over the Throne} as domination over it by subjugation and management, such that it does not move nor rest, nor is it particularized by the specific space which it occupies, nor is it described by any attribute at all except by the will of our Master, the Mighty and Majestic, and His creation of that in it. And the reason for specifying it in mention—even though all worlds equal it in what was mentioned of great need for al-Bārī, the Most High, and their not being independent of Him for a moment—is that since it is the greatest of created things, and the proportion of all of them to it is like a ring thrown in a desert, and perhaps it might be imagined that it has from strength and elevation what makes it independent in managing itself, so it was stated that it—despite its great strength and magnificent attributes—is subdued, in extreme need of our Master, the Mighty and Majestic, not possessing for itself nor for others harm nor benefit, nor does it manage its own affair, nor does any of creation besides Him, the Most High. And when that is established regarding it, it is even more established regarding others. And the verse has other well-known interpretations.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Haramayn's (al-Juwayni) ta'wīl of the verse of istiwā'. He interprets 'istawā' as istīlā' (domination) by means of subjugation and management (al-qahr wa al-tadbīr). He specifies that the Throne does not move or rest independently; all its motions and states are by Allah's will and creation. The passage then explains the wisdom behind specifically mentioning the Throne in this verse. Even though all created things are equally in need of Allah, the Throne is the greatest creation, and its grandeur might lead someone to imagine that it is self-sufficient. Therefore, the verse highlights that even the Throne is completely dependent on Allah. This argument is then extended: if the greatest creation is so dependent, then all else are even more so. The passage ends by noting that there are other well-known interpretations of the verse.

Key Terms
الاستيلاء
القهر
التدبير
الاستواء
العرش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the ta'wīl of istiwā' as istīlā' denies any attribute of istiwā' or implies that Allah is not truly described with it.
Correction: Actually, this ta'wīl affirms Allah's absolute control and management over the Throne, and it does not negate the attribute but interprets it in a manner befitting Allah's transcendence. The Throne's dependence is highlighted, not a denial of divine action.

Checkpoint
Why is the Throne specifically mentioned in the verse of istiwā' according to Imam al-Haramayn?
A) Because it is the only thing that needs Allah.
B) Because it is the greatest creation and might be thought independent.
C) Because it moves and rests.
D) Because it is the seat of Allah.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn interprets istiwā' as Allah's absolute domination over the Throne, highlighting that even the greatest creation is entirely dependent on Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Throne, the greatest creation, does not move, rest, or have any attribute except by Allah's will. When you move or rest today, will you remember that even your smallest action is created by Allah? What would change if you truly believed that?
?
The passage says the Throne is mentioned because one might imagine it is self-sufficient. In what area of your life do you most often act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting your utter dependence on Allah? How will you remind yourself of this truth today?
?
If the Throne—so magnificent—is in extreme need of Allah, then you are even more so. Does this thought make you feel humbled or anxious? How can you turn that feeling into a moment of sincere reliance on Allah right now?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
Imam al-Haramayn's Ta'wīl of Yad as Power
Sanusi presents Imam al-Haramayn's interpretation of 'yaday' as power with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Haramayn interprets the attribute of 'yad' (hand) in the Quran as power, based on Quranic and linguistic evidence.

Core Idea
Imam al-Haramayn interprets Allah's 'hands' as His power, not a literal limb.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a specific example of ta'wīl from Imam al-Haramayn. The attribute of 'yad' is interpreted as 'qudra' (power) using evidence from the Quran and Arabic usage. This demonstrates how classical scholars approach ambiguous verses about Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic source text and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms and the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse and Arabic idiom cited.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
Imam al-Haramayn provides two main evidences: a Quranic parallel (al-Dhariyat 47) and an Arabic linguistic idiom. Find both in the text.
The Quranic evidence uses 'ayd' (hands) meaning power, and the idiom uses 'yad' to mean ability.
3
Understand the rational support
The passage also adds a rational argument: the act of creation is already known to occur by power alone, so interpreting 'yaday' as power is consistent.
This reinforces the interpretation without contradicting other attributes.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this ta'wīl is important: it preserves God's transcendence while affirming a real attribute of power. Think about the difference between ta'wīl and tafwīḍ.
This lesson connects to broader discussions in kalam about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأوّل أيضاً إمام الحرمين (اليد) في قوله تعالى : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ بالقدرة ، على حدّ قولِهِ تعالى : وَالسَّمَاءَ بَنَيْنَهَا بِأَيْيْدٍ ﴾ [الذاريات : ٤٧]، ومِنْ كلامِ العربِ : ما لي بفلانٍ مِنْ يد؛ أي : مِنْ قدرةٍ وطاقة واعتضد بأنَّ الفعل قد تبيَّنَ أنَّ وقوعَهُ إنَّما هو بالقدرة دون غيرها
And Imam al-Haramayn also interpreted 'al-yad' (the hand) in His statement, the Most High: 'What I created with My two hands' as power, along the lines of His statement, the Most High: 'And the heaven We built with hands [quwwa]' [al-Dhariyat: 47], and from the speech of the Arabs: 'I have no hand in so-and-so' meaning no power and ability. And he supported [this] by the fact that the act has been clarified: its occurrence is only by power, not by anything else.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Haramayn's ta'wīl of the attribute of yad (hand) in the verse from Surat Sad. He interprets 'bi-yaday' (with My two hands) as meaning 'bi-qudratī' (with My power). He provides two pieces of evidence: first, a similar usage in the Quran where 'ayd' in Surat al-Dhariyat is interpreted as power; second, an Arabic idiom where 'yad' is used to mean power or ability. He further argues that the act of creation is already known to occur by power alone, so it is appropriate to interpret 'yaday' as power.

Misconception: That this ta'wīl denies the attribute of yad entirely or implies Allah is not omnipotent.
Correction: The ta'wīl actually affirms an attribute of power in a manner befitting Allah, preserving His transcendence while upholding His actions.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn's ta'wīl of 'yad' as power is supported by Quranic usage and Arabic idiom, affirming Allah's attribute of power in a transcendent sense.

?

The Ta'wil of 'Yad' as Power (Qudra)
Sanusi presents Imam al-Haramayn's ta'wil of 'yad' as qudra with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three pieces of evidence Imam al-Haramayn uses to interpret the attribute 'yad' as power (qudra) in the verse about Adam's creation.

Core Idea
The 'hand' of Allah is interpreted as power, as shown by Quran, language, and reason.

Imam al-Haramayn offers a careful interpretation of the divine attribute 'yad' (hand) that appears in the Qur'anic verse about Adam's creation. He does not take it literally but understands it as a reference to Allah's power. To support this, he brings together a parallel verse, a linguistic usage, and a rational argument. Let's examine each of these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi presents the ta'wil of Imam al-Haramayn.
The passage begins with 'Wa'awwala aydan'.
2
Identify the three proofs
Look for three distinct evidences: a Quranic parallel, an Arabic idiom, and a rational argument. Write them down briefly.
The rational proof is that action occurs only through power.
3
Reflect on the necessity of ta'wil
Ask yourself why the author argues that 'yad' must mean power. What would be the problem with a literal interpretation?
This lesson shows how kalam handles ambiguous attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأوّل أيضاً إمام الحرمين (اليد) في قوله تعالى : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ بالقدرة ، على حدّ قولِهِ تعالى : وَالسَّمَاءَ بَنَيْنَهَا بِأَيْيْدٍ ﴾ [الذاريات : ٤٧] ، ومِنْ كلامِ العربِ : ما لي بفلانٍ مِنْ يد ؛ أي : مِنْ قدرةٍ وطاقة واعتضد بأنَّ الفعل قد تبيَّنَ أنَّ وقوعَهُ إنَّما هو بالقدرة دون غيرها ، فوجبَ أنْ تكونَ اليد هنا بمعنى القدرةِ ، وإِنَّما أضاف آدم عليه السلام إلى القدرة وإنْ كانَتْ سائرُ الأفعالِ كذلك . . لتشريفه بذلك ونظير هذا التشريف : ما رُوِيَ عنه عليه الصلاةُ والسلام أَنَّهُ قالَ : خَلَقَ اللهُ آدَمَ بِيَدِهِ ، وَكَتَبَ التَّوْرَاةَ بِيَدِهِ ، وَغَرَسَ جَنَّةَ عَدْنٍ بیده (۱) ووجه هذا التشريف : أنَّ الله تعالى خلق آدم بقدرتِهِ ، ولم يُصَرِّف في خلقه أحداً مِنْ ملائكتِهِ كما فعل في غيرِهِ مِنْ ذُريَّتِهِ ؛ إِذْ وردَ في الحديثِ : « أَنَّ الْمَلَكَ يَأْخُذُ النُّطْفَةَ فِي الرَّحِم فَيَقُولُ : أَيْ رَبِّ ؛ ذَكَرٌ أَمْ أُنْثَى ؟ فَطِيمٌ أَمْ رَضِيعٌ ؟ مَا رِزْقُهُ ؟ وَمَا أَجَلُهُ ؟ (۲) ، وأَنَّ اللهَ تعالى سخَّرَ ما يشاءُ مِنْ ملائكتِهِ في النباتِ ، وفي غرس شجرِ الجنة ، ولا فاعل في الحقيقةِ إلا هو جلَّ وعزَّ ، ويكون هذا كقولنا : خاطَ الخائط ونَجَرَ النجَّارُ ، ولا فاعل للكلِّ إلا الله تعالى ، فكلُّ سكون أو حركة حلتْ في بدِ الصانع مِنْ بني آدم وغيرهم.. فإنَّ الله تعالى هو فاعلها وحدَهُ بلا واسطةٍ ، وكذا علومهم بها وقصدهم إليها .
And Imam al-Haramayn also interpreted (al-yad) in His statement: 'For what I created with My two hands' as power, on the order of His statement: 'And the heaven We built it with hands' (Q. 51:47), and from the speech of the Arabs: 'I have no hand (yad) over so-and-so', i.e., no power and ability. And he supported it by the fact that the action has been shown to occur only by power, not by anything else. So it is necessary that 'yad' here means power. And He only attributed Adam, peace be upon him, to power even though all other actions are likewise, to honor him by that. And an example of this honor is what was narrated from him (peace and blessings be upon him) that he said: 'Allah created Adam with His hand, wrote the Torah with His hand, and planted the Garden of Eden with His hand.' And the explanation of this honor is that Allah created Adam by His power and did not employ any of His angels in his creation as He did with others of his offspring, for it is reported in the hadith: 'Indeed the angel takes the drop in the womb and says: O Lord, male or female? Weaned or nursing? What is his provision? What is his lifespan?' And that Allah subjugated whatever He wills of His angels in plants and in planting the trees of Paradise. But there is no agent in reality except Him, Mighty and Majestic. And this is like our saying: 'The tailor sewed and the carpenter planed,' yet the agent of all is only Allah. So every stillness or motion that occurs in the body of the craftsman from among the sons of Adam and others – Allah alone is its agent without intermediary, and likewise their knowledge of it and their intention towards it.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the ta'wil of the attribute 'yad' (hand) in the verse 'created with My two hands' (Q. 38:75) as 'qudra' (power). The author provides three pieces of evidence: first, a parallel verse where 'ayd' means power (Q. 51:47); second, a linguistic usage where 'yad' in Arabic means power; third, a rational proof that actions occur through power alone. Therefore 'yad' here must mean power. Then the passage addresses why Adam is specifically attributed to Allah's 'yad' even though all actions are similarly by Allah's power. This is for tashrif (honorific distinction) – to honor Adam. The hadith about creating Adam, writing the Torah, and planting Paradise with 'His hand' are cited as examples. The explanation is that Allah directly created Adam without using angels as intermediaries, unlike the creation of his descendants where angels are involved. This shows that angels are mere instruments while Allah is the sole true agent. The author then generalizes: every motion or stillness in any agent's body is directly created by Allah, and the agent's knowledge and intention are also created by Him. This reinforces the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition) and that Allah is the sole creator of all actions.

Key Terms
اليد
القدرة
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think 'yad' in the Quran refers to a literal hand of Allah.
Correction: This passage shows that 'yad' is interpreted as power based on Quranic, linguistic, and rational evidence, avoiding anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the proofs Imam al-Haramayn uses to interpret 'yad' as power?
A) A Quranic verse where 'ayd' means power.
B) An Arabic idiom where 'yad' means hand.
C) A rational argument that actions occur only through power.
D) A linguistic usage from Arab speech.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn proves that 'yad' in the creation verse means power through combined textual and rational evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'created with My two hands', what image forms in your heart? Are you willing to abandon that image for the sake of Allah's transcendence, even if it feels less tangible? What will you do the next time such a verse stirs your imagination?
?
This lesson shows that scholars rely on evidence from the Quran, language, and reason to interpret divine attributes. Are you personally committed to following this disciplined methodology, or do you prefer your own intuition? How will you train yourself to submit to sound scholarship?
?
If you were asked to defend Allah's transcendence to someone who insists on a literal hand, would your trust in this ta'wil hold firm? What does your answer reveal about the strength of your conviction in the Ash'ari approach?

Honorific Creation of Adam and True Agency
Sanusi explains the honorific attribution of Adam to Allah's power and the doctrine of sole divine agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Adam's creation is specifically attributed to Allah's 'yad' as an honor, and how this leads to the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah is the sole true agent of all actions.

Core Idea
Adam is honored by direct creation without angelic intermediaries; all real agency belongs to Allah.

Having established that 'yad' means power, Sanusi now addresses a subtle point: why is Adam's creation specifically attributed to Allah's 'yad' when all actions are equally by His power? The answer lies in honorific distinction. This then opens into a broader discussion about the role of angels and the true nature of agency, culminating in the Ash'ari principle that Allah alone is the real agent behind every action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on tashrif
Read the Arabic passage from 'Wa innama adafa' to the end. Identify the reason why Adam is singled out.
The key term is 'tashrif' (honor).
2
Analyze the hadith and angelic role
The hadith about creation of Adam, Torah, and garden, and the angelic involvement in creation of offspring. Notice how angels are instruments.
Angels are intermediaries but not creators.
3
Grasp the principle of true agency
The passage concludes that every movement and stillness is directly from Allah, and human actions are merely acquired (kasb). Understand the analogy of tailor and carpenter.
This reinforces the Ash'ari view of divine omnipotence.

The Meaning of 'Yad' in Creation: Power and Honor
Sanusi explains that 'yad' means power and God is the sole agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'yad' in the creation narrative is interpreted as power and how this relates to God's sole agency.

Core Idea
God's 'hand' is power, and He alone is the true agent of all actions.

Sanusi addresses a key theological point: when the Quran and hadith mention God's 'hand' in creating Adam, it must be understood figuratively as power, not a physical limb. He explains that the specific attribution to Adam is for honor, as God created him directly without angelic intermediation. This passage also establishes that God is the sole creator of all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Note that Sanusi uses a hadith as evidence.
The passage is dense with theological reasoning.
2
Understand why 'yad' must mean power
Sanusi asserts that 'yad' in this context must be interpreted as power (qudra) because attributing a physical hand to God is anthropomorphic. The context of creation supports this figurative meaning.
This is a standard Ash'ari interpretation to maintain divine transcendence.
3
Recognize the honorific attribution
Adam is specifically attributed to God's power even though all actions depend on God. The reason is honor (tashrif): God created Adam directly without employing angels, as indicated by the hadith.
The honor distinguishes Adam from other humans where angels mediate.
4
Grasp the principle of God's sole agency
Sanusi extends the argument: every motion and stillness in creation is directly caused by God. Human actions are metaphors; God is the sole true agent (al-fāʿil al-ḥaqīqī).
This is a key Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ووجه هذا التشريف : أنَّ الله تعالى خلق آدم بقدرتِهِ ، ولم يُصَرِّف في خلقه أحداً مِنْ ملائكتِهِ كما فعل في غيرِهِ مِنْ ذُريَّتِهِ ؛ إِذْ وردَ في الحديثِ : « أَنَّ الْمَلَكَ يَأْخُذُ النُّطْفَةَ فِي الرَّحِم فَيَقُولُ : أَيْ رَبِّ ; ذَكَرٌ أَمْ أُنْثَى ؟ فَطِيمٌ أَمْ رَضِيعٌ ؟ مَا رِزْقُهُ ؟ وَمَا أَجَلُهُ ؟ (۲) ، وأَنَّ اللهَ تعالى سخَّرَ ما يشاءُ مِنْ ملائكتِهِ في النباتِ ، وفي غرس شجرِ الجنة ، ولا فاعل في الحقيقةِ إلا هو جلَّ وعزَّ ، ويكون هذا كقولنا : خاطَ الخائط ونَجَرَ النجَّارُ ، ولا فاعل للكلِّ إلا الله تعالى ، فكلُّ سكون أو حركة حلتْ في بدِ الصانع مِنْ بني آدم وغيرهم.. فإنَّ الله تعالى هو فاعلها وحدَهُ بلا واسطةٍ ، وكذا علومهم بها وقصدهم إليها .

إضافة
So it must be that 'the hand' here means power, and He only attributed Adam (peace be upon him) to power, even though all other acts are likewise, for his honor. And analogous to this honor is what has been narrated from him (peace and blessings be upon him) that he said: 'God created Adam with His hand, and wrote the Torah with His hand, and planted the Garden of Eden with His hand' (1). And the explanation of this honor is that God Almighty created Adam with His power, and did not employ any of His angels in his creation, as He did with others of his offspring; for it has come in the hadith: 'The angel takes the sperm in the womb and says: O Lord, male or female? Weaned or suckling? What is his provision? What is his lifespan?' (2), and that God Almighty subjugated whom He wills of His angels in vegetation, and in planting the trees of Paradise, and there is no agent in reality except Him, Mighty and Majestic. And this is like our saying: 'The tailor sewed and the carpenter planed,' and there is no agent for all except God Almighty. So every stillness or motion that occurs in the hand of the craftsman from the children of Adam and others, indeed God Almighty is its agent alone without intermediary, and likewise their knowledge of them and their intention towards them. And from this meaning — that the intent in attribution is honor — addition.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation of the divine attribute 'yad' (hand) as it appears in the creation of Adam. The author, Sanusi, begins by stating that the term 'yad' in this context must be interpreted as 'power' (qudra). He then notes that Adam is specifically attributed to God's power even though all actions are equally dependent on God, and the reason is to honor Adam (tashrif). To support this, he cites a hadith where the Prophet says God created Adam with His hand, wrote the Torah with His hand, and planted the Garden of Eden with His hand. The author explains that the honor consists in God creating Adam directly by His power without employing any angel, unlike the rest of humanity where angels are involved in the womb. He references the hadith of the angel asking God about the fetus. He then extends the principle: God subjugates angels for vegetation and planting, but ultimately God is the sole true agent (la faʿila fī al-ḥaqīqa illā huwa). He draws an analogy: when we say 'the tailor sewed', the true agent is God, as the movements of the tailor are created by God. Similarly, all motion and stillness in human actions are directly created by God. Thus, the attribution of 'hand' to God is an honorific addition indicating direct divine action without intermediate cause.

Key Terms
اليد (al-yad)
القدرة (al-qudra)
التشريف (al-tashrif)
الفاعل الحقيقي (al-fāʿil al-ḥaqīqī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'yad' refers to a physical hand of God.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies it means power, maintaining divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is Adam specifically attributed to God's 'hand' (power) even though all acts are from God?
A) Because Adam was created without parents
B) To honor Adam by emphasizing God's direct creation without angelic mediation
C) Because Adam's body was made from clay
D) To distinguish Adam from angels

Key Takeaway
The term 'yad' for God means power, and attributing it to Adam honors him by indicating God's direct creation without intermediate angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that God is the sole agent of all actions, including your every movement and stillness. When you succeed, do you truly credit God, or do you subtly attribute it to your own skill? When you fail, do you blame circumstances, forgetting that even your failure is under God's direct control? Today, commit to saying 'Masha'Allah' for every outcome, recognizing His sole agency.
?
You are a descendant of Adam, but unlike Adam, you were formed through a process involving angels. Does that make you less honored? Or does it challenge you to see the hand of God even in the ordinary? Reflect on your own birth and ask yourself: Do I live as if I am a product of chance or as a direct creation of God's will? What would change if you truly believed every detail of your existence is crafted by Him alone?
?
The concept of 'yad' as power rather than a limb is a call to purify your conception of God. In what hidden ways do you still imagine God with human limitations? When you pray, do you picture God 'hearing' with an ear or 'seeing' with an eye? Take a moment to repent of any anthropomorphism, and ask Allah to increase your awe of His transcendence. Then, write one practical way you will remind yourself of His true nature today.

The Torah and Garden: Direct Divine Action as Miracle
Sanusi explains that the Torah and Garden are attributed to God's hand to indicate direct action and miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that attributing the Torah and Garden to God's hand signifies direct divine action and miraculous nature, without angelic intermediation.

Core Idea
Direct divine action is shown through miracles like the emerald Torah tablets.

Sanusi deepens our understanding of divine attributes by examining specific cases: the Torah and the Garden of Eden. He argues that when texts say 'written by My hand' or 'planted by My hand', it means God directly performed these acts as miracles, without using angels. This lesson unpacks that meaning and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to how Sanusi uses the example of the Torah and the Garden.
Notice the three parts: Torah, emerald tablets, Garden.
2
Understand the key distinction
Sanusi distinguishes between God acting directly and using angels. Identify where he mentions angels being used or not used.
God creates the writer and writing; angels may transmit scriptures.
3
Analyze the miracle of the emerald tablets
The tablets are said to be of green emerald and entirely letters, which is a miracle because emerald cannot normally be engraved.
This is a clear example of khāriq al-ʿāda.
4
Reflect on the Garden of Eden
Similarly, the Garden was planted directly by God's power without angels. This reinforces the theme of direct divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتب التوراة إلى يدِهِ تعالى ؛ أي : قدرتِه ، والمعنى : أنَّه سبحانه وتعالى تولَّى أمرها مِنْ غيرِ أنْ يُصرِّفَ فيها أحداً مِنَ الملائكة ، وقد يُصرِّفُ الله سبحانه الملائكة في غير هاذا ؛ كما في نَقْلِ الصُّحُفِ مِنَ اللوح المحفوظ ، وكَتْبِ الصحفِ على العبادِ ، والله جل وعزَّ هو الخالقُ للكاتب والكتابة ؛ وهي حركات الكاتب وسكناته .
وقد ورد في مأثورِ الأخبارِ : أَنَّ الألواح التي كتب الله تعالى فيها التوراة كانَتْ مِنْ زمرُّذٍ أخضر (۱) ، وكانَتْ حروفاً كلُّها ، ظاهرها وباطنها . وهو مِنْ خوارقِ العادةِ ; لأنَّ الزمرد لا ينتقشُ في جزي العادة ، ولا يرتسم فيه رسم .
وكذا خصّ الله تعالى جَنَّاتِ عدن بأنْ غرس شجرَها بقدرتِهِ دونَ أَنْ يتعبد بغرسها أحداً من الملائكة
The writing of the Torah is attributed to His hand, may He be exalted, that is, His power. And the meaning is that He, glorified and exalted, undertook its affair without employing any of the angels in it. And God, glorified, may employ angels in other than this, as in the transmission of the scriptures from the Preserved Tablet, and the writing of records upon the servants. And God, mighty and majestic, is the Creator of the writer and the writing — which are the movements and stillnesses of the writer. And it has come in transmitted reports that the tablets on which God Almighty wrote the Torah were of green emerald (1), and were entirely letters, both their exterior and interior. And this is among miraculous anomalies (khawāriq al-ʿāda), because emerald is not engraved in the part [i.e., nature] of habit, nor does an impression become traced on it. Likewise, God Almighty distinguished the Gardens of Eden by planting their trees with His power, without employing any of the angels to plant them.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the divine attribute 'yad' by focusing on the Torah and the Garden of Eden. Sanusi explains that the phrase 'the Torah is written by His hand' means by His power, and that God directly undertook its writing without angelic involvement, though He may employ angels for other tasks like transmitting scriptures from the Preserved Tablet or writing records. He emphasizes that God is the Creator of both the writer and the act of writing, as all movements and stillnesses are created by Him. He then mentions a transmitted report that the tablets of the Torah were made of green emerald and were entirely letters on both sides, which is a miracle (khāriq al-ʿāda) because emerald naturally cannot be engraved. Similarly, the Garden of Eden was planted directly by God's power without angels. Thus, these attributions signify miracles and God's direct creative action.

القدرة (al-qudra)
خوارق العادة (khawāriq al-ʿāda)
الألواح (al-alwāḥ)
الزمرذ (al-zumurrudh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The emerald tablets are a literal physical description.
Correction: Sanusi uses the emerald tablets to demonstrate the miraculous nature of God's direct action, as emerald cannot normally be engraved.

God's direct action without intermediaries is a sign of His power and is categorized as miraculous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Wisdom of the Dual 'Yadayn' for Adam
Sanusi explains the wisdom behind the dual 'yadayn' for Adam, involving power and blessing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the wisdom behind the dual form 'two hands' in reference to Adam, and how it reflects both God's power and blessing while also alluding to Iblis.

Core Idea
The dual form 'yadayn' merges God's power and blessing for Adam.

Sanusi addresses a subtle point in the Quranic description of Adam's creation using the dual 'yadayn.' He explains that this is not a literal attribution of two hands to God, but a linguistic device combining two distinct meanings—power and blessing. This explanation also contains a gentle reprimand to Iblis. Let's explore the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces the wisdom (ḥikma) and then gives his probable explanation.
The passage includes a footnote marker (2) which is not part of the original text.
2
Identify the two 'hands'
Sanusi mentions two meanings of 'hand': the hand of power (yad al-qudra) and the hand of blessing (yad al-niʿma). Understand how each applies to Adam's creation.
In Arabic, 'hand' is used metaphorically for power and blessing.
3
Understand the linguistic principle
Sanusi notes that in Arabic, the dual form only requires agreement in wording, not necessarily identical meaning. This allows combining two different concepts under one dual term.
This principle is key to avoiding literal anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حكمة تثنية ( اليدين ) : فيحتمل - والله تعالى أعلمُ - أَنَّهُ جَلَّ وعزَّ خلق آدم بقدرتِهِ ، وهي يد في لغة العرب، وأنعم عليه بنعمة الهداية وغيرها ممَّا هو كثير لا ينحصر ، والنعمة أيضاً يد في لغة العرب ، فجاءَتِ التثنية باعتبارِ بدِ القدرة ويدِ النعمة ، بناءً على أنَّهُ لا يُشترط في التثنية إلا مُجرَّدُ الاتفاق في اللفظ (٢)
ويكون فيه تعريض بإبليس ؛ بأنَّهُ لم تصحبه في خلقه اليدان ،
And as for the wisdom of the dual (two hands): it is probable — and God Almighty knows best — that He, mighty and majestic, created Adam with His power, and power is called 'hand' in the language of the Arabs, and He bestowed upon him the blessing of guidance and other countless blessings, and blessing is also called 'hand' in the language of the Arabs. So the dual came to combine the hand of power and the hand of blessing, based on the principle that for the dual, only mere agreement in wording is required (2). And in it is an allusion to Iblis, in that he was not accompanied by both hands in his creation.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage examines the wisdom behind the use of the dual form 'yadayn' (two hands) in reference to Adam. Sanusi offers a probable explanation: God created Adam with His power (qudra), which is called 'hand' in Arabic, and He also granted him blessings like guidance, which are also called 'hand' in Arabic. Thus, the dual form combines these two meanings — the hand of power and the hand of blessing. He notes that in Arabic, duality merely requires agreement in wording, not necessarily that the two nouns are identical in essence. Additionally, he sees an allusion to Iblis (Satan), who, unlike Adam, was not created with both hands (i.e., was not honored with the combination of power and blessing). This reinforces the honorific nature of the dual attribution to Adam.

Key Terms
اليدان (al-yadayn)
التثنية (al-tathniya)
بد القدرة (yad al-qudra)
يد النعمة (yad al-niʿma)
إبليس (Iblīs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the dual 'yadayn' indicates two literal hands for God or that Adam was created with two physical hands of God.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that 'yad' is metaphorical for power and blessing, and the dual form combines these two meanings linguistically, not literally.

Checkpoint

A) To indicate two literal hands
B) To combine the hand of power and the hand of blessing
C) To show that Adam is preeminent over angels
D) To emphasize God's omnipotence

Key Takeaway
The dual 'yadayn' for Adam combines the hand of power and the hand of blessing, highlighting Adam's honor and contrasting with Iblis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been given both the hand of power (your very existence) and the hand of blessing (guidance and countless favors). Are you truly living as if you have received both? Which hand do you take for granted more?
?
Iblis was deprived of the dual honor because of his arrogance. When you read that Allah created Adam with both power and blessing, do you feel a sense of gratitude or do you, like Iblis, presume that you are self-sufficient?
?
The dual 'yadayn' requires only phonetic agreement, not semantic unity. In your own life, do you sometimes demand that God's actions be consistent with your limited understanding? Will you accept that His wisdom may combine things that seem mismatched to you?

The Hand of God as Power and the Miracle of the Emerald Tablets
Sanusi explains the metaphor of God's hand as power and cites the miracle of the emerald tablets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the 'hand of God' in Islamic theology refers to His power, not a literal limb, and that the emerald tablets are a miracle demonstrating God's direct action without angelic intermediation.

Core Idea
God's hand is His power; emerald tablets are a miracle of direct divine writing.

In this passage, Sanusi explains a common metaphorical expression in Islamic theology: 'God's hand.' He clarifies that it means God's power, not a literal hand. He further illustrates God's direct action with the miraculous emerald tablets of the Torah and the planting of Eden's trees. This helps us understand how kalam interprets anthropomorphic expressions and supernatural events.

The Wisdom of the Dual Hands in Creation
Sanusi explains the wisdom of dual hands and contrasts Adam with Iblis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and theological wisdom behind the dual mention of God's hands in relation to Adam and the contrast with Iblis.

Core Idea
The dual hands combine power and blessing, contrasting Adam's honored creation with Iblis's deprivation.

This passage delves into the meaning of the 'dual hands' mentioned in the context of Adam's creation. Sanusi explains that the duality is not literal but linguistic, representing two distinct divine attributes: power and blessing. He then uses this to draw a profound contrast with Iblis, who received only power without blessing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the author's meaning first-hand.
Notice the footnote marker (2) embedded in the text.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'hand'
Find where Sanusi defines the two hands: one as power, the other as blessing. Understand the linguistic principle that duality can be based on mere verbal agreement.
This is a key theological method—avoiding literalism.
3
Understand the allusion to Iblis
See how the dual hands contrast Adam (both power and blessing) with Iblis (only power, no blessing). Reflect on why this matters for theodicy.
4
Consider the broader lesson
Ask yourself: what does this teach about God's justice and mercy? How does it address the problem of evil (Iblis's wretchedness)?
This connects the passage to kalam's larger concerns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حكمة تثنية ( اليدين ) : فيحتمل - والله تعالى أعلمُ - أَنَّهُ جَلَّ وعزَّ خلق آدم بقدرتِهِ ، وهي يد في لغة العرب ، وأنعم عليه بنعمة الهداية وغيرها ممَّا هو كثير لا ينحصر ، والنعمة أيضاً يد في لغة العرب ، فجاءَتِ التثنية باعتبارِ يد القدرة ويدِ النعمة ، بناءً على أنَّهُ لا يُشترط في التثنية إلا مُجرَّدُ الاتفاق في اللفظ (٢)
ويكون فيه تعريض بإبليس ؛ بأنَّهُ لم تصحبه في خلقه اليدان ،
وإِنَّما صحبته يد واحدةٌ ؛ وهي القدرة ؛ لأنَّهُ إِنَّما خَلَقَهُ اللهُ تعالى بقدرتِهِ ، ولم ينعم عليه بهدايتِهِ وإسعادِهِ ، بل هو ممَّنْ حُتِّمَتْ عليهِ الشقاوة في الأزلِ ، والزمَ سَيِّئَ الاعتقادِ مِنْ مبدأ نشأته إلى حلول الأجلِ :
As for the wisdom of the dual (hands) (tathniyat al-yadayn): it is possible—and God knows best—that He, majestic and exalted, created Adam with His power, which is 'hand' in the Arabic language, and bestowed upon him the blessing of guidance and other countless favors, and the blessing is also 'hand' in Arabic. So the duality came to indicate both the hand of power and the hand of blessing, based on the principle that nothing is required for duality except mere agreement in wording (2). And in this is an allusion to Iblis, because he was not accompanied by the two hands in his creation; rather, he was accompanied by only one hand, which is power, because God created him with His power and did not bestow upon him His guidance and felicity. Rather, he is among those upon whom wretchedness was decreed in pre-eternity, and he was compelled to adopt evil belief from the beginning of his existence until the end of his term.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage explores the wisdom behind the dual mention of God's hands in certain contexts, such as in the creation of Adam. The author proposes that the two hands refer to the hand of power (creating Adam) and the hand of blessing (the favors bestowed). This is not a literal duality but a linguistic agreement. The allusion to Iblis is that he only had the hand of power (creation) without the hand of blessing (guidance), which explains his wretchedness and evil belief.

The First Ignorance: Bodies Are Not Inherently Different
Sanusi refutes Iblis's first ignorance about bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutation of Iblis's first ignorance: that bodies are equal in essence and differentiation comes only from God's choice.

Core Idea
Bodies are not inherently superior; only God's choice makes them so.

Sanusi begins by exposing Iblis's first ignorance: the philosophical error that bodies are inherently different in nature. This lesson unpacks the Ash'ari refutation, which affirms that all bodies are equal in essence and that God freely assigns attributes to any body He wills.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces Iblis's background before stating the first ignorance.
2
Identify the first ignorance
Find the sentence that states Iblis's belief about bodies being inherently different. Understand what 'inherently different' means here.
This is the core error that Sanusi refutes.
3
Understand the refutation
Read the Ash'ari response: bodies are equal in essence, and God arbitrarily distinguishes some with attributes. Note the examples given (hot/cold, luminous/dark, living/dead).
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that bodies are containers for attributes, and no body is superior except by God's preference. This refutes the philosophical ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد قيل : إنَّ الشيطان قد عبد الله تعالى ثمانين ألف سنةٍ لا يفتر ، لكن قد صحبَهُ في عبادتِهِ جهالتانِ كُتبتا عليهِ في الأزلِ ، ولا حول ولا قوَّةَ إلا بالله العلي العظيم ، وهما أمارتا شقاوته :
إحداهما : أنَّ الأجسامَ كانَتْ عندَهُ متفاضلة بطباعها : وهي جهالة فلسفية لا ثبوت لها على مذهب المؤمنينَ ، وإِنَّما العالم كله عندهم أجسام وصفاتُ أجسام ، لا فضل لبعضه على بعضٍ مِنْ حيثُ ذاتُهُ (۱) ، فيُخصِّصُ الله تعالى أي جسم شاءَ بأي صفةٍ شاءَ ، ويُبدِّلُ الحار بارداً ، والمنير مظلماً ، والحيَّ ميتاً إلى غير ذلك ، فالأجسام ظروف وأوعيةٌ للصفاتِ ، ومولانا جلَّ وعزَّ مختار لكلِّ ظرف منها ما يجعلُ فيهِ ، فلا أفضليَّةَ لجسم على آخرَ إلا بتفضيله سبحانه وتعالى
الجهالة الثانيةُ : أَنَّهُ كانَ يعتقد أنَّ قدرة العبدِ لها أثر في الأفعالِ وأنَّ العبد إنَّما يطيع الله تعالى أو يعصيه بقدرتِهِ ومشيئتِهِ هو ، لا أنَّ اللهَ تعالى هو الذي يخلقُ الطاعة والمعصية ، ونشأ لهُ مِنْ هذه الجهالة اعتقاد وجوبِ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح على الله تعالى ، وهذه جهالة القدريّة مجوس هذه الأمة
It has been said: Iblis worshipped God for eighty thousand years without tiring, but he was accompanied in his worship by two ignorances that were decreed for him in pre-eternity—and there is no power or might except by God, the Exalted, the Magnificent—and they are the signs of his wretchedness. The first: that bodies, in his view, are inherently different in their natures. This is a philosophical ignorance with no foundation according to the belief of the believers. Rather, the entire world, in their view, consists of bodies and attributes of bodies, with no preference of one over another in terms of their essence (1). So God distinguishes any body He wishes with any attribute He wishes, and He changes the hot to cold, the luminous to dark, the living to dead, and so on. Thus, bodies are containers and vessels for attributes, and our Lord, majestic and exalted, chooses for each container what He places in it. There is no superiority of one body over another except by His preference, glorified and exalted. The second ignorance: that he believed that the servant's power has an effect on actions, and that the servant obeys God or disobeys Him by his own power and will, not that God creates obedience and disobedience. From this ignorance arose his belief in the obligation of considering what is good and best (al-salah wa al-aslah) upon God. And this is the ignorance of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this community.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage details the two ignorances of Iblis that led to his wretchedness despite long worship. The first ignorance is philosophical: the belief that bodies are inherently different in nature. The refutation from the Ash'ari perspective is that all bodies are equal in essence, and differentiation comes solely from God's choice. The second ignorance is theological: the belief that human power has an effect on actions, which leads to the Qadariyya error of obligating God to do what is best. This classifies Iblis as a Qadari, which the author calls the Magians of this community.

Key Terms
جهالتان
أجسام
تفاضل بالطباع
صفات الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some bodies are inherently better than others by their nature.
Correction: All bodies are equal in essence; God freely assigns attributes and preferences.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the first ignorance of Iblis?
A) That bodies are inherently different in nature
B) That the servant's power has an effect on actions
C) That God must observe what is good and best
D) That angels are superior to humans

Key Takeaway
All bodies are equal in essence; differentiation comes solely from God's will, not from inherent nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look down on someone because of their appearance, wealth, or background, are you repeating Iblis's first ignorance—assuming some bodies are inherently superior? How can you actively remind yourself today that all bodies are equal in essence before God?
?
In what area of your life have you believed that certain material things (your job, your home, your health) are 'better' by their nature, rather than seeing them as mere containers for God's choice? What would change if you truly internalized that God alone gives value?
?
Iblis worshipped for thousands of years, yet his ignorance made his worship worthless. What hidden assumptions in your own heart might be making your good deeds hollow before God? Ask Him to expose them and replace them with truth.

The Second Ignorance: Servant's Power and the Qadariyya Error
Sanusi refutes Iblis's second ignorance about human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Iblis's second ignorance: that human power affects actions, leading to the Qadariyya belief that God must do what is best.

Core Idea
Human power does not create actions; only God creates obedience and disobedience.

Sanusi now turns to Iblis's second ignorance: the belief that the servant's power has an effect on actions and that the servant acts independently. This lesson shows how this error leads to the Qadariyya doctrine, which Sanusi condemns as the Magians of the community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the link Sanusi makes between the second ignorance and the Qadariyya.
2
Identify the second ignorance
Find the sentence stating that Iblis believed the servant's power has an effect on actions and that the servant obeys or disobeys by his own power and will.
3
Trace the derivation
Sanusi says this ignorance leads to the belief that God must observe al-salah wa al-aslah. Understand why: if humans create their own actions, then God's justice would require Him to always choose what is best for them.
This derived belief is also false in Ash'ari theology.
4
Recognize the sectarian label
Sanusi calls the Qadariyya the 'Magians of this community.' Reflect on why he uses such strong language.
Magians are dualists; Qadariyya attribute creation to humans alongside God.

Allah's Hands and Eye: Literal or Metaphorical?
Sanusi explains metaphorical interpretation of Allah's hands and eye.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari scholars interpret divine attributes like 'hands' and 'eye' that appear in the Quran, either literally (haqiqa) or metaphorically (majaz), and why metaphorical interpretation is permissible to preserve Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
Affirming divine attributes while avoiding anthropomorphism is possible through metaphorical interpretation.

The Quran describes Allah with attributes like 'hands' and 'eye.' How should Muslims understand such descriptions? Do they mean actual physical body parts? In this passage, the great Ash'ari theologian Sanusi explains that these can be understood metaphorically, such that 'hands' refer to power and 'eye' to knowledge or protection, preserving Allah's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation. Notice how the passage treats the dual form of 'hands' and the interpretation of 'eye'.
The text ends mid-verse, but the point is clear.
2
Understand literal vs metaphorical distinction
Sanusi presents two possibilities: literal understanding of the dual 'hands' or metaphorical. If metaphorical, 'hands' refer to power. Why is the dual used? For magnification.
This is a key concept in kalam: using figurative language to exalt Allah.
3
Examine interpretation of 'eye'
The verse 'under My eye' is interpreted as knowledge or protection, not a literal eye. This shows the Ash'ari method of ta'wil (figurative interpretation) when a literal meaning would imply corporeality.
This aligns with the principle of tanzih (transcendence).
4
Consider broader significance
This passage demonstrates how to balance affirming the attributes mentioned in the Quran with rejecting anthropomorphism. The metaphorical reading is not a denial but a deeper understanding.
Ask yourself: Why would the dual/plural be used for a single attribute? For emphasis and exaltation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا كلُّهُ إِنْ فهمنا التثنية في اليدين على الحقيقة، وأما إنْ فهمناها على المجاز، وأنَّ المراد بها إنَّما هو القدرة.. فإنَّما تُنّي القدرة وإنْ كانَتْ واحدةً لتعظيمها بالتثنية؛ كما يُعظَّم الشيء الواحد بالتعبير عنه بالجمع. وأما (العين) في قوله: وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي ﴾ [طه: ٣٩]: فأُوِّلَتْ بالعلم، أو بالكلاءة والحفظ، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ تَجْرِي
And all this, if we understand the dual in the two hands literally, but if we understand it metaphorically, and that what is meant by it is only power... then power is dualized although it is one, to magnify it by the dual, just as a single thing is magnified by expressing it in the plural. And as for (the eye) in His saying: {And that you be formed under My eye} [Taha: 39]: it is interpreted as knowledge, or as protection and preservation, and likewise His saying, the Exalted: {running}
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the interpretation of two divine attributes mentioned in the Quran: the 'hands' and the 'eye'. The author presents two possible understandings: literal (haqiqa) or metaphorical (majaz). He states that if we take the dual 'hands' metaphorically, it refers to power (qudra). Although power is one, it is expressed in the dual form to magnify it, similar to the use of the plural for a single item to show greatness. Then the author discusses the word 'eye' in the Quranic verse from Surah Taha (20:39): 'And that you be formed under My eye.' He notes that this is interpreted either as knowledge ('ilm) or as protection/safekeeping (kila'a wa hifz). The passage ends mid-sentence, beginning to mention another verse ('running') but the text is cut off. Throughout, the author demonstrates the standard Ash'ari approach of affirming the attributes while avoiding anthropomorphism by figurative interpretation when necessary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that taking 'hands' and 'eye' literally is necessary to affirm the attributes.
Correction: Ash'ari theology allows metaphorical interpretation to preserve tanzih (transcendence) while still affirming the attributes.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Sanusi uses a linguistic analogy: the dual form magnifies a single thing, just as the plural honors a single entity. Think of a time when you used language to magnify or honor someone. In what ways do you honor Allah in your daily speech? Will focusing on the rhetorical depth of Quranic attributes make your worship more deliberate and less mechanical? What specific phrase will you meditate on today?

Interpreting 'al-Wajh' in Q. 55:27 as Existence
Author argues that 'al-wajh' means existence, critiquing Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author interprets 'al-wajh' in Q. 55:27 as existence rather than an attribute, and the two key arguments against the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
In the Qur'an, God's 'face' means His existence, not a separate attribute.

This lesson examines a specific theological interpretation of a Qur'anic verse. When Allah says 'And there will remain the Face of your Lord,' what does 'face' mean? The author argues it refers to God's very existence, and he provides two powerful reasons why taking it as an attribute is problematic. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then follow with the English translation. Notice the author's direct engagement with the verse and the Ash'ari view.
The passage includes a footnote marker (۱) — don't be distracted by it.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The author gives two reasons why interpreting 'al-wajh' as an attribute is unlikely. Find them: one about the nature of attributes, the other about the description 'Possessor of Majesty'.
3
Understand the first argument
Attributes are not inherently everlasting; only God's essence is eternal. The reply that attribute's permanence implies permanence of subject is forced.
This argument guards against multiple eternal entities.
4
Understand the second argument
The verse describes 'al-wajh' as 'Possessor of Majesty'. But an attribute cannot be described by attributes; majesty belongs to God's essence.
If 'al-wajh' were an attribute, it would be described by another attribute — which is impossible.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
The author's interpretation preserves God's unity and avoids positing eternal attributes as separate entities.
This is a key point in Islamic theology about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ( الوجه ( في قولِهِ تعالى : ( وَيَبْقَى وَجْهُ رَبِّكَ ذُو الْجَلَلِ وَالْإِكْرَامِ ) الرحمن : ۲۷] : فأوَّلَهُ الإمام بالوجود ؛ أي : ويبقى وجود ربَّكَ ذو الجلال والإكرام ، وهو ذاته سبحانَهُ ، ويبعد حمْلُ الوجه على صفةٍ من صفاتِهِ تعالى كما يقوله الشيخ الأشعري ؛ لعدم اختصاص صفتِهِ تعالى بالبقاء (۱) ، بل هو جلَّ وعلا الواجب القدم والبقاء بذاتِهِ وجميعِ صفاتِهِ ، إلا أنْ يُجابَ لهُ بأنَّ بقاء الصفةِ يستلزم بقاء الموصوفِ وسائرِ صفاتِهِ ، وفيهِ معَ ذلكَ تكلُّفٌ وممَّا يدلُّ أيضاً على أنَّ المراد بـ ( الوجهِ ( الوجودُ : نعتُهُ بأَنَّهُ ذو الجلال والإكرام ، والموصوفُ بالجلالِ هو الله سبحانه وتعالى ؛ لأنَّ الجلال هو صفاتِ الربوبية ، فلو أُريد بـ اتصافه جلَّ وعزَّ بجميع ( الوجهِ ) صفةٌ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى .. لم يُتصوَّرْ أَنْ تُوصف بصفاتٍ ؛ إذ الصفة لا تقوم بها صفةٌ .
As for the word 'al-wajh' in His saying 'And there will remain the Face of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor' (Q. 55:27): the Imam interpreted it as existence; i.e., the existence of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor, will remain, and He is His Essence. Interpreting al-wajh as one of His attributes, as Sheikh al-Ash'ari says, is unlikely; because His attribute is not specifically qualified with remaining, rather He is the necessarily pre-eternal and everlasting in His Essence and all His attributes. Unless one replies that the permanence of the attribute entails the permanence of the described object and all its attributes, but in that there is still some artificiality. What also indicates that the intended meaning of 'al-wajh' is existence: its description as 'Possessor of Majesty and Honor', and the one described by majesty is Allah, because majesty is the attributes of lordship. So if by 'al-wajh' a single attribute of His attributes were intended... it would not be conceivable for it to be described with attributes, because an attribute does not subsist in an attribute.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage provides a theological interpretation of the word 'al-wajh' in Q. 55:27. The author reports that the Imam (likely referring to a leading scholar such as Imam al-Ash'ari or another, but here the author criticizes the Ash'ari view) interpreted 'al-wajh' as existence, meaning that what remains is God's existence, which is His essence. The author argues that taking 'al-wajh' as an attribute like the Ash'ari's position is improbable for two reasons. First, attributes are not inherently everlasting; only God's essence has eternal subsistence. Even if one argues that the attribute's permanence implies the permanence of the subject and all attributes, this is forced. Second, the verse describes 'al-wajh' as possessing majesty (jallal) and honor (ikram), but it is God who is properly described by majesty. An attribute cannot itself be described by other attributes, because an attribute does not subsist in another attribute. The passage thus defends a monotheistic understanding that avoids positing multiple eternal entities.

Key Terms
الوجه
الوجود
الجلال والإكرام
الصفة
الربوبية
الشيخ الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Wajh' in Q. 55:27 is a separate attribute of God.
Correction: The author argues it means God's existence itself, based on the nature of attributes and the verse's description.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it unlikely that 'al-wajh' in Q. 55:27 is an attribute of God?
A) Because the word 'wajh' cannot be used for attributes
B) Because an attribute is not everlasting itself, and it cannot be described by attributes
C) Because the Imam said so
D) Because the verse is not authentic

Key Takeaway
The term 'al-wajh' in Q. 55:27 refers to God's existence, not a separate attribute, based on the nature of attributes and the verse's description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about God's 'face' or 'hands', do you tend to imagine a physical attribute or do you immediately seek the intended meaning that befits His majesty? What does your default reaction reveal about how much you guard your understanding of Tawhid?
?
The author calls the alternative interpretation 'forced'. Are there areas of your own faith where you hold onto comfortable interpretations despite logical or scriptural weakness? What would it cost you to abandon them for a sounder understanding?
?
If you were to explain to someone that God's 'face' in Q. 55:27 is His existence, not a part or attribute, how would your own conviction affect the way you live in His presence? Does your daily awareness of God reflect that He is not composed of parts, but is simply and perfectly One?

First Interpretation of 'Everything Is Perishing'
Author presents the first of three interpretations of Q. 28:88.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first interpretation of Q. 28:88, which explains 'halik' as 'mortal' so that the verse affirms that every living being dies except Allah.

Core Idea
The first interpretation equates 'perishing' with death for living things, reserving eternity for Allah alone.

This lesson unpacks the first of three classical interpretations of the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' The author clarifies that 'halik' here means 'mortal' or 'dying', drawing on another Quranic usage. This interpretation restricts the sense to living beings, asserting that all life ends except God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get the literal meaning. Note the key terms and the Quranic analogy.
2
Understand the lexical move
Notice how the author uses Q. 4:176 ('if a man perishes') to show that 'halik' can mean 'died'. This is his evidence for the interpretation.
This shows the importance of Quranic usage in tafsir.
3
Grasp the doctrinal point
The interpretation implies that only living things are subject to 'halik' as death, and that God alone is the living one who does not die. This affirms God's eternal life.
Contrast this with other interpretations that may include non-living things.

Source Text

This passage begins an enumeration of three interpretations of Q. 28:88. The author lists the first interpretation: the word 'halik' is understood as 'mortal' or 'dying', drawing an analogy with the Quranic usage of 'halaka' meaning 'died'. Thus, the verse means every living being is subject to death except God. This interpretation treats 'wajh' as referring to God Himself, not a separate attribute. The remaining two interpretations are not present in this page but are implied.
