Answering an Objection to the Third Proof
Sanusi answers an objection to the third proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes an objection to the third proof by demonstrating that the objection presupposes an impossible infinite regress of temporal events.

Core Idea
An infinite sequence of temporal attributes is just as impossible as a single one.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a clever objection to the third proof (which argued that a temporal attribute in God would imply deficiency). The objector suggests that God could have always had a succession of such attributes, avoiding any deficiency. Sanusi's response is sharp: this objection merely repackages the impossibility of an infinite regress of events, which has already been disproven. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that if God is eternal, He could have been described by a series of temporal attributes in succession without a beginning, so no single attribute is ever 'missing' in pre-eternity.
This is an attempt to avoid the conclusion that deficiency would arise.
2
Identify Sanusi's rebuttal
Sanusi points out that this objection is exactly the concept of 'temporal events without beginning' (حوادث لا أول لها), which has already been proven impossible.
The impossibility of an infinite regress of accidents was established earlier in the creed.
3
Connect to the broader argument
See how Sanusi uses a previously proven principle to refute the objection, showing that the objection is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا اعتراض على البرهان الثالث

و تقريرُهُ أَنْ يُقال : ما ذكر تُموهُ مِنْ لزومِ النقص له تعالى على تقدير اتصافه تعالى بصفةٍ حادثة .. غيرُ مُسلَّم ، وقولكم في بيان لزوم ذلك : ) لأنَّ تلك الصفةَ لا بدَّ وأنْ تكون صفة كمال ، وقد فاتتِ الذات في الأزلِ ، وفوتُ الكمال نقص ) . . لا يصحُ ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : لمَّا كانَتْ ذاتُهُ عزَّ وجلَّ أزليَّةً فما المانع أنْ يُقالَ باتصافه قبل تلك الصفة الحادثة المفروضة بأمثالها على التعاقبِ لا إلى أوَّلِ ، فلا يفوت حينئذ الذات كمال هذه الصفة - لا في الأزلِ ولا فيما لا يزالُ - حتى يلزم النقصُ ؟ والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض : ما أشرنا إليه في أصل العقيدة مراراً ؛ مِنْ أنَّ هذا الاحتمال الذي اعترض بهِ المعترِضُ على الدليل باطل قطعاً ؛ لأنَّ ذلك الاحتمال هو عين إثباتِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها وقد سبق برهان استحالته (۱)
This is an objection to the third proof. Its formulation is that it is said: What you mentioned of the necessity of deficiency for Him, Most High, on the assumption that He, Most High, is described by a temporal attribute, is not conceded. And your statement in explaining the necessity of that: 'Because that attribute must be an attribute of perfection, and the essence lacked it in pre-eternity, and lacking perfection is deficiency' is not valid; because we say: Since His essence, Mighty and Majestic, is eternal, what prevents it being said that He is described before that supposed temporal attribute by similar ones in succession without a first? Then the essence does not lack the perfection of this attribute – neither in pre-eternity nor in what never ceases – so that deficiency would be necessary? The answer to this objection is what we have indicated in the original creed repeatedly: that this possibility with which the objector has objected to the proof is absolutely false; because that possibility is precisely the affirmation of events without beginning, and the proof of its impossibility has already preceded (1).
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector challenges the third proof (which likely argued that a temporal attribute in God would imply deficiency). The objector claims: if God is eternal, why could He not have been described by similar attributes in succession without beginning, so that no deficiency occurs? Sanusi replies that this objection is invalid because it assumes an infinite series of temporal events (حوادث لا أول لها), which has already been proven impossible. Thus, the objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector thinks that an infinite succession of temporal attributes avoids deficiency and is possible.
Correction: An infinite succession of temporal attributes is equivalent to an infinite regress of accidents, which has been proven impossible.

Checkpoint

A) God cannot have any attributes.
B) God could have been described with similar attributes in succession without beginning, so no deficiency occurs.
C) The third proof is circular.
D) All attributes of God must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The objection that God could have had an infinite succession of temporal attributes is invalid because an infinite regress of temporal events is impossible.

Oneness of Each Divine Attribute and Its Proof
Sanusi proves the oneness of each divine attribute and classifies attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why each of God's attributes must be one and the proof based on the impossibility of two similar attributes coexisting.

Core Idea
Each attribute of God is one; multiplicity would lead to impossible consequences.

This passage from Sanusi establishes a foundational principle of divine attributes: each attribute (like knowledge or life) is one, not multiple. He provides a logical proof and distinguishes between attributes that relate to something other than the essence (related) and those that do not (non-related). The proof for non-related attributes is explained in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the meaning. Notice the key terms: اجتماع المثلين (coexistence of two similar things) and تحصيل الحاصل (attainment of the attained).
[]
2
Understand the main rule and its proof
The rule: every attribute of God must be one. The proof: if an attribute were multiple, it would lead to the coexistence of two similar attributes in one essence, which is impossible. Also, it would lead to تحصيل الحاصل (redundancy).
This is an a priori rational proof.
3
Learn the classification of attributes
Attributes are divided into two kinds: non-related (like life) which only require a locus, and related (like knowledge and power) which relate to objects. This classification affects the proof.
[]
4
Study the proof for non-related attributes
For non-related attributes like life, multiplicity would mean two lives in one essence, leading to union (اتحاد) of two things into one, which is absurd.
The passage ends here; the proof for related attributes is implied but not fully given.
5
Reflect on the misconception
A common misunderstanding is that God could have multiple attributes of the same kind (e.g., two sciences). This fallacy is prevented by the rational argument.
[]

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب الوحدة الصفات المعاني ]

ويلزمُ أنْ تكونَ كلُّ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى واحدةً ، وإلا لزم اجتماع المثلين ، وتحصيل الحاصل ، وهو محال

يعني

: أنَّه يجبُ في كلِّ صفةٍ مِنَ الصفات التي تقوم بذاتِ مولانا جل وعزَّ ؛ كالعلم والقدرة ونحوهما أن تكون واحدةً ، فيعلمُ جلَّ وعزَّ جميع المعلوماتِ التي لا نهاية لها بعلم واحد ، ويقدر على جميع المقدوراتِ التي لا نهاية لها بقدرةٍ واحدةٍ ، وقس على هذين ما بقي مِنَ الصفات

والدليل على وجوبِ الوَحدةِ لكلَّ واحدةٍ مِنْ هذه الصفاتِ : ما أشرنا إليه في أصل العقيدة ؛ وهو أنَّهُ لو كانَتْ صفةٌ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى متعدّدةً ؛ كأنْ يُقَدَّرَ أَنَّهُ تعالى يعلمُ المعلوماتِ بعلوم مُتعدّدةٍ ، أو يقدرُ على المقدوراتِ بقُدَرٍ مُتعدّدةٍ ، أو يريد المرادات بإرادات متعددة ، ويُقَدَّرَ مثلُ ذلك في سائر الصفاتِ .. لزم على هذا التقديرِ اجتماع

المثلين .

وبيانُ اللزوم : وغيرُ مُتعلقة

مثلاً .

: أن تعرفَ أوَّلاً أنَّ الصفاتِ على ضربين : مُتعلّقة

فغيرُ المُتعلِّقةِ : ما لا يطلب سوى المحلَّ الذي يقومُ بهِ ؛ كالحياة

والمتعلقة : خلافُ ذلكَ ؛ كالعلم والقدرة ونحوهما

•

أما لزوم اجتماع المثلين على تقدير التعددِ في الصفات غير المتعلقة : فواضح ؛ إذْ لو كانَ لهُ تعالى حياتان ، وقد عرفت أنَّ التركيب في ذاتِهِ العليَّة مستحيل .. للزم اجتماع حياتين في محل واحد ضرورةً ، وذلك مستحيل ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ الاتحاد ؛ وهو صيرورة [؟]
[The obligation of oneness of the attributes of meanings] And it is necessary that every attribute of His attributes, Most High, be one, otherwise the coexistence of two similar things and the attainment of the attained would be necessary, and that is impossible. Meaning: It is obligatory that every one of the attributes that subsist in the essence of our Lord, Mighty and Majestic, such as knowledge, power, and the like, be one. So He, Mighty and Majestic, knows all infinite known things with one knowledge, and has power over all infinite possible things with one power, and analogize to these two the rest of the attributes. The proof for the obligation of oneness for each one of these attributes is what we have indicated in the original creed: that if an attribute of His attributes, Most High, were multiple – e.g., if He, Most High, were supposed to know known things with multiple knowledges, or have power over possible things with multiple powers, or will objects of will with multiple wills, and the like were supposed in the rest of the attributes – then on this supposition the coexistence of two similar things would be necessary. And the explanation of the necessity: and you should first know that attributes are of two kinds: related and non-related. The non-related: that which requires only the locus in which it subsists, like life. The related: the opposite of that, like knowledge, power, and the like. As for the necessity of the coexistence of two similar things on the supposition of multiplicity in non-related attributes: it is clear. For if He, Most High, had two lives, and you have known that composition in His sublime essence is impossible, then the coexistence of two lives in one locus would necessarily follow, and that is impossible; because it necessitates union, which is the becoming [of two things into one?].
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the oneness of each divine attribute. It begins by stating the rule: every attribute of God must be one, otherwise two similar attributes would coexist (اجتماع المثلين) and the attained would be attained again (تحصيل الحاصل), both impossible. Then it explains that attributes like knowledge and power are each single and cover all objects. The proof: if an attribute were multiple, e.g., multiple knowledges, that would require two identical attributes in one subject, which is impossible. The passage then classifies attributes into non-related (like life) and related (like knowledge). For non-related attributes, multiplicity is impossible because two lives in one essence would require union (اتحاد). The passage ends before completing the explanation for related attributes, but the overall argument is established.

Key Terms
وحدة الصفات
اجتماع المثلين
تحصيل الحاصل
صفات متعلقة
صفات غير متعلقة
الحياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for God to have multiple attributes of the same kind, e.g., two knowledges or two lives.
Correction: Multiplicity of the same kind in one subject leads to the absurdity of two identical attributes coexisting (اجتماع المثلين) or redundancy (تحصيل الحاصل). This is rationally impossible, as Sanusi proves.

Checkpoint
Why is multiplicity of non-related attributes like life impossible in God?
A) Because they would cancel each other out.
B) Because two lives in one essence would require union.
C) Because life is not an attribute of God.
D) Because God's life is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Every attribute of God must be one; multiplicity leads to the impossible coexistence of two similar things in one essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God knows everything with one single knowledge. Are there moments in your life where you doubt that God fully knows your situation, as if He needs multiple knowledges to grasp your complexity? How will you remind yourself that His one knowledge is sufficient and perfect?
?
The proof against multiple attributes shows that even the possibility of multiplicity would lead to absurdity. Yet, in your daily worship, do you ever attribute different 'versions' of God's mercy or anger based on your actions? How can you recalibrate your so that you see His attributes as perfectly unified, not fragmented?
?
If God's power is one and covers all things, then your greatest difficulty is within His grasp. But if you still feel helpless, where does that feeling come from? What step will you take today to align your trust in God with the truth that His one power is enough for everything?

Classification of Attributes and Oneness of Non-Relational Ones
Sanusi classifies attributes and proves oneness for non-relational ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of divine attributes (relational and non-relational) and why non-relational attributes must be one.

Core Idea
Non-relational attributes cannot be multiple because multiple identical attributes in one subject necessarily unite.

Imam Sanusi begins by stating that each divine attribute must be one. He then establishes a crucial classification: some attributes are relational, relating to external objects, while others are non-relational, requiring only a subject. By focusing on non-relational attributes like life, he demonstrates that multiplicity would lead to an impossible union.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the central claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that the opening states the conclusion: each attribute must be one. Underline the key terms for relational and non-relational attributes.
The passage is dense; read slowly to absorb the structure.
2
Focus on the classification of attributes
Identify the two kinds: non-relational (like life) and relational (like knowledge). Understand the difference: non-relational only need a subject, while relational also have an object (mutʿallaq).
3
Work through the proof for non-relational attributes
The proof shows that if there were two lives, they would be in the same subject (Allah), and being identical with no other differentiator, they would unite—which is impossible. Follow each step: multiplicity → same subject → no difference → union → impossibility.
The term 'unification' (ittiḥād) here means two things becoming one, not a mystical merging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جل وعزَّ ؛ كالعلم والقدرة ونحوهما أن تكون واحدةً ، فيعلمُ جلَّ وعزَّ جميع المعلوماتِ التي لا نهاية لها بعلم واحد ، ويقدر على جميع المقدوراتِ التي لا نهاية لها بقدرةٍ واحدةٍ ، وقس على هذين ما بقي مِنَ الصفات .
والدليل على وجوبِ الوَحدةِ لكلَّ واحدةٍ مِنْ هذه الصفاتِ : ما أشرنا إليه في أصل العقيدة ؛ وهو أنَّهُ لو كانَتْ صفةٌ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى متعدّدةً ؛ كأنْ يُقَدَّرَ أَنَّهُ تعالى يعلمُ المعلوماتِ بعلوم مُتعدّدةٍ ، أو يقدرُ على المقدوراتِ بقُدَرٍ مُتعدّدةٍ ، أو يريد المرادات بإرادات متعددة ، ويُقَدَّرَ مثلُ ذلك في سائر الصفاتِ .. لزم على هذا التقديرِ اجتماع المثلين .
وبيان اللزوم : أن تعرفَ أوَّلاً أنَّ الصفاتِ على ضربين : مُتعلّقة وغير مُتعلقة .
فغير المُتعلِّقةِ : ما لا يطلب سوى المحلَّ الذي يقومُ بهِ ؛ كالحياة .
والمتعلقة : خلافُ ذلكَ ؛ كالعلم والقدرة ونحوهما .
أما لزوم اجتماع المثلين على تقدير التعددِ في الصفات غير المتعلقة : فواضح ؛ إذْ لو كانَ لهُ تعالى حياتان ، وقد عرفت أنَّ التركيب في ذاتِهِ العليَّة مستحيل .. للزم اجتماع حياتين في محل واحد ضرورةً ، وذلك مستحيل ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ الاتحاد ؛ وهو صيرورة الشيئين شيئاً واحداً ؛ إذ الصفات المتماثلةُ إِنَّما تتغايرُ بحَسَبِ اختلافِ المحلَّ أوِ المتعلَّقِ أو الزمانِ ، وإذا انتفى التغاير لزم الاتحاد ، وهو باطل على ما سبق .
And [it is necessary] that His attributes, such as knowledge and power and the like, be one. So He, may He be exalted, knows all infinite known things with one knowledge, and has power over all infinite possible things with one power. And analogize to these two the remaining attributes. The proof for the necessity of oneness for each of these attributes is what we pointed to in the original creed: that if an attribute of His, exalted, were multiple—such as if He were supposed to know known things with multiple knowledges, or have power over possibles with multiple powers, or will willed things with multiple wills, and the like is supposed in the rest of the attributes—then this supposition would entail the conjunction of similars (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn). The explanation of the entailment: you must first know that attributes are of two kinds: relational (mutaʿalliqa) and non-relational (ghayr mutaʿalliqa). The non-relational is that which seeks only the locus (al-maḥall) in which it inheres, like life. The relational is the opposite of that, like knowledge and power and the like. As for the entailment of the conjunction of similars on the supposition of multiplicity in non-relational attributes, it is clear: for if He, exalted, had two lives, and you know that composition in His sublime essence is impossible, then the conjunction of two lives in one locus would necessarily follow, and that is impossible because it entails unification (al-ittiḥād), which is the becoming of two things into one thing; for identical attributes only differ according to difference of locus, relation, or time, and if differentiation is negated, unification follows, and that is invalid as established previously.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the conclusion that each divine attribute must be one. Then it lays out the general proof: if any attribute were multiple, it would entail the conjunction of similars (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn), which is impossible. To explain this entailment, the author first classifies attributes into two types: non-relational (ghayr mutaʿalliqa) and relational (mutaʿalliqa). Non-relational attributes, like life, only require a subject (maḥall) to inhere in; they do not relate to objects outside themselves. Relational attributes, like knowledge and power, do relate to objects. The proof for non-relational attributes is straightforward: if there were two lives, they would inhere in the same subject, but since the subject is one and indivisible, the two lives would be identical in every respect (same subject, no relation, no time difference), leading to their actual union (ittiḥād). This union is impossible because it would mean two things becoming one, contradicting their multiplicity. Therefore, multiple non-relational attributes cannot exist. The author then applies this reasoning to all non-relational attributes.

Key Terms
صفات متع (ṣifāt mutaʿalliqa)
صفات غير متع (ṣifāt ghayr mutaʿalliqa)
اجتماع المثلين (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn)
الاتحاد (al-ittiḥād)
المحل (al-maḥall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'unification' (ittiḥād) means the attributes merge into one another in a mystical sense.
Correction: Sanusi uses ittiḥād in a logical sense: two identical attributes in the same subject become indistinguishable, which contradicts the assumption that they are distinct and multiple. It is not a merging but a failure of differentiation.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is it impossible for Allah to have two lives?
A) Because life is a relational attribute and cannot be multiple.
B) Because two lives would require two subjects, but Allah is one.
C) Because two lives in one subject would lead to their unification, contradicting multiplicity.
D) Because life is a created attribute and cannot apply to Allah.

Key Takeaway
The singularity of non-relational attributes is proven by the impossibility of two identical attributes uniting in one subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah's knowledge is one, yet it encompasses every detail of your life—every thought, every action, every hidden motive. Do you live as if He knows every moment of your day? What would change if you truly internalized that His single knowledge sees all, without division or distraction?
?
The proof shows that multiple identical attributes would lead to unification—God's simplicity is absolute. Your own heart is often divided, pulled between different desires and loyalties. In what ways do you need to ask Allah to simplify your intentions and unify your purpose toward Him alone?
?
If Allah's life is one and non-relational, it means He is not dependent on anything—yet you are utterly dependent on Him. When you feel self-sufficient, where are you actually placing your trust? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that your very existence depends on the One who lives without need?

Oneness of Relational Attributes: Argument from Conjunction of Similars
Sanusi proves oneness of relational attributes via conjunction of similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why each of Allah's relational attributes must be singular, based on the impossibility of two identical attributes in the same subject.

Core Idea
A second knowledge would be identical to the first—impossible conjunction of similars.

Sanusi now tackles relational attributes like knowledge and power. Since these attributes have infinite objects, any second attribute of the same kind would have no distinct object, leading to an impossible redundancy. This argument establishes the oneness of each relational attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's logic unfold.
The passage references an earlier proof about infinite objects.
2
Understand the premise
Sanusi reminds us that relational attributes have no limit on their objects—they relate to infinite known things.
This is key: no second attribute can have a different scope.
3
Follow the supposition
Suppose there were a second knowledge. It would have to relate to the same infinite objects as the first.
4
See the consequence
The two knowledges would be perfectly similar in object, subject, and time, leading to the conjunction of similars, which is impossible.
The same applies to power, will, and other relational attributes.
5
Extend the analogy
Sanusi says to analogize the remaining attributes—so think about power, will, etc.
This shows the argument is general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما لزوم اجتماع المثلينِ في الصفاتِ المُتعلقة : فقد عرفت بالبرهان فيما سبق أنَّ صفاتِهِ تعالى المتعلقة تستحيلُ النهاية في متعلقاتِها ، بل هي تتعلَّقُ بما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ المعلوماتِ ، فالعلمُ الواحد له تعالى قد سبق أنَّه يجب أن يتعلَّق بما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ المعلوماتِ . فلو فُرِضَ أَنَّ لَهُ تعالى علماً آخر.. لوجب أن يتعلَّق بمثل ما تعلَّق بهِ الأَوَّلُ ، فيتماثلان ، ومحلُّهما واحدٌ لا تعدُّدَ فيه ، فقد لزم اجتماع المثلين ، وقس على هذا باقي الصفاتِ . واستدل أيضاً في أصل العقيدة على وجوبِ الوَحدة لصفاتِهِ تعالى : بأنَّهُ لو تعدَّدَتْ لزم تحصيل الحاصل ، ولزوم ذلك واضح ؛ لأنَّهُ لو كانَتْ لهُ تعالى حياتان أو علمان مثلاً .. لكان أحد العلمين أو إحدى الحياتين : إمَّا أَنْ يُحصّلا للذات ما هو لازم لها ؛ وهو كونُ الذاتِ حيَّةٌ عالمةً ، ولا شكّ أنَّ ذلك تحصيل لما حصل للذاتِ ؛ لحصول ذلكَ لها بالحياة الأخرى والعلم الآخر .
As for the entailment of the conjunction of similars in relational attributes: you have known from the proof above that His attributes, exalted, that are relational are impossible of ending in their objects, rather they relate to what has no end of known things. Thus the one knowledge of Him, exalted, as has preceded, must relate to what has no end of known things. So if it were supposed that He, exalted, has another knowledge, it would necessarily relate to the same as what the first related to, so they would be similar, and their locus is one with no multiplicity, so the conjunction of similars would necessarily follow. And analogize to this the remaining attributes. He also argued in the original creed for the necessity of oneness for His attributes, exalted: that if they were multiple, it would entail obtaining the already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), and the necessity of that is clear; because if He, exalted, had two lives or two knowledges, for example, then one of the two knowledges or one of the two lives would either achieve for the essence what is necessary for it, which is that the essence is living and knowing, and there is no doubt that this is obtaining what has already been obtained for the essence due to its being obtained through the other life and the other knowledge.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage covers the proof for the oneness of relational attributes and an additional argument. The author first addresses relational attributes (like knowledge and power). He reminds the reader that these attributes have infinite objects—Allah's knowledge encompasses all infinite known things. If there were a second knowledge, it would necessarily relate to the same infinite set of objects as the first, making the two knowledges identical in object, subject, and time (since they are eternal). Thus they would be perfect similars in one subject, leading to the impossible conjunction of similars. The same applies to power, will, etc. The author then presents a second, independent proof: if there were two attributes of the same kind (e.g., two lives), each would achieve for the divine essence the state of being living. But the essence is already living through the other life, so the second life would be achieving something already achieved (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. This second argument applies to both relational and non-relational attributes. The author thus establishes that each attribute must be one.

Key Terms
اجتماع المثلين (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn)
الصفات المتعلقة (al-ṣifāt al-mutaʿalliqa)
المتعلق (al-mutaʿallaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That relational attributes can be multiple because they differ in their objects—but since each relational attribute already encompasses all possible objects, a second would have no distinct object.
Correction: The first proof shows that a second attribute would relate to the same objects, causing conjunction of similars.

Checkpoint
Why would a second divine knowledge be impossible according to the first proof?
A) Because two knowledges would be temporally distinct.
B) Because the second knowledge would have no object to relate to.
C) Because it would have to relate to the same objects as the first, causing conjunction of similars.
D) Because knowledge is a non-relational attribute.

Key Takeaway
Each relational attribute of Allah must be one because a second would be identical to the first, leading to the impossible conjunction of similars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that Allah's one knowledge encompasses every single detail of your life—past, present, future, and all possible scenarios—how does that change the way you speak to Him in du'a? Are you truly convinced that He already knows your deepest needs, or do your repeated requests reveal a doubt that He might not have noticed? Commit today to pray with the certainty that His one knowledge has already accounted for every tear and every silent hope.
?
The proof shows that a second knowledge would be redundant—identical to the first. In your own life, where have you been seeking 'another knowledge' besides Allah's? Perhaps you rely on your own plans, the advice of others, or worldly systems as if they know better than the All-Knowing. Identify one area where you have acted as if you need a 'second knowledge' to fix things, and repent by entrusting it wholly to His single, perfect knowledge.
?
Sanusi's argument extends to power and will: a second power or will would also be identical to the first, making multiplicity impossible. Have you ever imagined that Allah's will could change—that He might regret or revise His decree? This lesson demands that you abandon any notion of variability in His attributes. Are you at peace with the fact that His will is one, unchanging, and already perfectly fulfilled? If not, what does that say about your submission to His decree?

Oneness of Attributes: Argument from Obtaining the Obtained
Sanusi gives a second proof for attribute oneness: tahsil al-hasil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the oneness of divine attributes: that multiple attributes of the same kind would redundantly achieve what is already achieved, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Two lives or knowledges would each make the essence alive—but it's already alive.

Sanusi presents an additional, independent argument. Even if the conjunction of similars didn't apply, having two attributes of the same kind would be pointless because the first already fully achieves the effect. This argument applies to both relational and non-relational attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Carefully read the second part of the passage, starting with 'واستدل أيضاً'.
This is a separate proof from the first.
2
Identify the supposition
Suppose there were two lives or two knowledges.
The example uses life and knowledge.
3
Understand the effect
Each life makes the essence 'living'; each knowledge makes it 'knowing'.
These are necessary concomitants of the attributes.
4
Recognize the redundancy
The essence is already living through the other life, so the second life would be achieving something already achieved.
This is tahsil al-hasil, which is absurd.
5
Generalize the proof
The same applies to all attributes: a second would be useless.
This proof is simpler but powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And analogize to this the remaining attributes. Commentary
This passage covers the proof for the oneness of relational attributes and an additional argument. The author first addresses relational attributes (like knowledge and power). He reminds the reader that these attributes have infinite objects—Allah's knowledge encompasses all infinite known things. If there were a second knowledge, it would necessarily relate to the same infinite set of objects as the first, making the two knowledges identical in object, subject, and time (since they are eternal). Impossibility of Two Similar Attributes
Sanusi proves the impossibility of two similar attributes in one subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two similar attributes cannot coexist in one subject, particularly in the case of God's attributes.

Core Idea
Two similar attributes in one subject would be identical.

This passage tackles a key theological problem: can God have multiple similar attributes, like two knowledges? Sanusi argues that such a scenario leads to logical contradiction. We'll follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Learn the rule that similar attributes only differ by subject, object, or time. Without such differences, they become identical.
This is the foundation of the argument.
2
Apply to God's attributes
Consider God's related attributes, like knowledge. They relate to infinite objects, so any two would have the same objects and subject.
3
Conclude the impossibility
Realize that two such attributes would meet the criteria for identity, leading to the impossibility of having two similar attributes in one subject.
This refutes the possibility of multiple similar divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيئين شيئاً واحداً ؛ إذ الصفات المتماثلةُ إِنَّما تتغايرُ بحَسَبِ اختلافِ المحلَّ أوِ المتعلَّقِ أو الزمانِ ، وإذا انتفى التغاير لزم الاتحاد ، وهو باطل على ما سبق (۱)
وأما لزوم اجتماع المثلينِ في الصفاتِ المُتعلقة : فقد عرفت بالبرهان فيما سبق أنَّ صفاتِهِ تعالى المتعلقة تستحيلُ النهاية في متعلقاتِها ، بل هي تتعلَّقُ بما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ المعلوماتِ ، فالعلمُ الواحد له تعالى قد سبق أنَّه يجب أن يتعلَّق بما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ
المعلومات (۲)
فلو فُرِضَ أَنَّ لَهُ تعالى علماً آخر.. لوجب أن يتعلَّق بمثل ما تعلَّق بهِ الأَوَّلُ ، فيتماثلان ، ومحلُّهما واحدٌ لا تعدُّدَ فيه ، فقد فقد لزم اجتماع المثلين ، وقس على هذا باقي الصفاتِ
[It would mean] the two things are one thing; because similar attributes only differ according to difference of subject, object, or time. When difference is absent, union follows, and that is invalid as previously established. As for the necessity of the conjunction of similars (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn) in related attributes (al-ṣifāt al-mutaʿalliqa): you have known by proof previously that His attributes—exalted is He—that are related are impossible of having an end in their objects, rather they relate to what has no end of known things. So the one knowledge of Him—exalted is He—has been previously established that it must relate to what has no end of known things. If another knowledge were supposed for Him—exalted is He—it would have to relate to the like of what the first related to, so they would be similar, and their subject is one with no multiplicity in it, so indeed the conjunction of similars would necessarily follow. And apply the same to the rest of the attributes.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the possibility of multiple similar attributes in God, specifically addressing the issue of 'اجتماع المثلين' (conjunction of two similars). The author begins by stating a principle: similar attributes can only differ by virtue of difference in their subject, object, or time. If no such difference exists, they become identical, which is invalid. Then he applies this to the related attributes (الصفات المتعلقة), which are infinite in their objects. If there were two knowledges, they would each relate to the same infinite objects, making them similar and their subject one, thus they would be identical (union). This leads to the impossibility of two similar attributes in one subject. The argument is then extended to all attributes.

اجتماع المثلين
صفات متعلقة
لا نهاية
علم
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think two similar attributes can coexist if they differ in their objects.
Correction: In God, objects are infinite, so any two knowledges would relate to the same infinite set, making them similar and their subject one, leading to identity.

Checkpoint

B) They would be identical due to same subject and infinite objects
C) They would cause redundancy
D) They would require a specifier

Key Takeaway
Multiple similar attributes in God are impossible because they would be identical, as they share the same subject and infinitely similar objects.

The Argument from Redundancy for Unity of Attributes
Sanusi proves the unity of attributes by showing that multiplicity leads to either redundancy or impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument from redundancy (تحصيل الحاصل) that proves the necessity of unity in the divine attributes.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of attributes leads to absurdity: either redundancy or impossibility.

Sanusi presents a concise argument to demonstrate that the divine attributes must be one. The argument uses a dilemma: if there were multiple similar attributes, each would either produce its necessary effect or not. Both options are impossible. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the dilemma structure: 'إما ... وإما'.
The argument is built on the concept of 'تحصيل الحاصل' (obtaining what is already obtained).
2
Understand the two possibilities
The second half presents the two horns of the dilemma: either each attribute brings about the necessary effect (leading to redundancy) or it does not (leading to impossibility). Grasp why both are unacceptable.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Since both possibilities are absurd, the assumption of multiple attributes must be false. Therefore, each attribute (life, knowledge, etc.) is necessarily one.
This argument is decisive in kalām for unity of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل أيضاً في أصل العقيدة على وجوبِ الوَحدة لصفاتِهِ تعالى : بأنَّهُ لو تعدَّدَتْ لزم تحصيل الحاصل ، ولزوم ذلك واضح ؛ لأنَّهُ لو كانَتْ لهُ تعالى حياتان أو علمان مثلاً .. لكان أحد العلمين أو إحدى الحياتين : إِمَّا أَنْ يُحصّلا للذات ما هو لازم لها ؛ لازم لها ؛ وهو كونُ الذاتِ حيَّةٌ عالمةٌ ، ولا شكّ أنَّ ذلك تحصيل لما حصل للذاتِ ؛ لحصول ذلكَ لها بالحياة الأخرى والعلم الآخرِ
(١) تقدم ( ص ٢٤٢) (۲) تقدم ( ص ٣٤١ ) .
وإِمَّا ألا يُحصلا للذاتِ ذلكَ اللازم ، فيلزم أن يكونا وُجِدا بدونِ لازمهما الذي يستحيلُ أنْ يُوجَدا عاريين عنه ، وذلك كلُّهُ لا يُعقَلُ
He also argued in the treatise on creed (Aṣl al-ʿAqīda) for the necessity of unity for His attributes—exalted is He: that if they were multiple, obtaining what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) would follow, and the necessity of that is clear. Because if He—exalted is He—had two lives or two knowledges, for instance, each of the two knowledges or each of the two lives would either bring about for the essence what is necessary for it—necessary for it—which is that the essence is living and knowing, and there is no doubt that that is obtaining what has already been obtained for the essence, because that has already been obtained for it by the other life and the other knowledge; or they would not bring about that necessary for the essence, so they would necessarily exist without their necessary concomitant, which it is impossible for them to exist devoid of. And all of that is unintelligible.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from 'تحصيل الحاصل' (obtaining what is already obtained) to prove the necessity of unity of divine attributes. The argument is presented as a dilemma: if there were two lives or two knowledges, either each attribute brings about the necessary effect (the essence being living/knowing) or not. If it does, then it is redundant since the effect is already achieved by the other, thus it is 'obtaining what is already obtained,' which is absurd. If it does not bring about the effect, then the attribute exists without its necessary concomitant, which is impossible. Therefore, multiplicity is impossible, and the attributes must be one.

Key Terms
تحصيل الحاصل
وحدة الصفات
لازم
ذات
حياة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think multiple identical attributes could have distinct effects or be necessary for different reasons.
Correction: The argument shows that the effect of life is simply 'being living' – a single state. Any additional life would either repeat that effect (redundancy) or not produce it (impossible). So multiplicity is logically excluded.

Checkpoint
In the argument from 'تحصيل الحاصل', what is the second possibility given for multiple attributes?
A) They bring about the same effect
B) They fail to bring about the necessary effect
C) They divide the objects among themselves
D) They are infinite

Key Takeaway
The argument from taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil shows that multiplicity in divine attributes is logically impossible because it leads to either redundancy or a missing essential effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's knowledge is a single, all-encompassing reality, or do you sometimes unconsciously imagine He needs multiple 'pieces' of information? How might that hidden assumption weaken your trust in His perfect decisions?
?
If you were to explain this argument to a friend who doubts the unity of Allah's attributes, where in your own heart do you find resistance to accepting it? What would it cost you to let go of the idea that multiple attributes could be more 'powerful'?
?
The argument shows that redundancy (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) is absurd in Allah. But in your own life, do you ever multiply efforts or thoughts about Allah's attributes that are already sufficient? How can this lesson push you to be satisfied with the perfect oneness of His knowledge and life?

Refuting Multiplicity of Related Attributes: Two Impossibilities
Sanusi refutes multiplicity of related attributes by showing both horns lead to impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both infinite multiplicity and finite multiplicity covering infinite objects are impossible, thereby establishing the oneness of Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
Any multiplicity of related attributes leads to impossibility, whether infinite or finite.

Sanusi presents a decisive argument against multiplicity in the attributes that relate to objects. He analyzes the hypothetical scenario into two exhaustive possibilities—either each object gets its own attribute (infinite attributes) or a finite number of attributes cover all objects. Both possibilities are shown to be impossible, proving that multiplicity cannot occur. This lesson walks through the logic of both horns.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the two possibilities Sanusi presents. Note the conclusion that both are impossible.
2
Identify the two horns
The passage breaks down into two cases: attributes multiply with objects (infinite number) or they do not (finite number covering infinite objects). Recognize this structure.
3
Understand why infinite multiplicity fails
Sanusi argues that since objects are infinite, having an attribute for each would require an infinite number of attributes, which is impossible in reality.
The impossibility lies in the entry of an infinite number into existence.
4
Understand why finite covering infinite fails
A finite number of attributes cannot cover infinite objects equally unless there is a specifier; otherwise, discrimination would require a specifier that leads to contingency (ḥudūth), which is impossible for the eternal.
This highlights the need for a mukhaṣṣiṣ (specifier) and the problem of contingency.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since both possibilities are impossible, multiplicity of related attributes is impossible. This supports the doctrine of tawḥīd of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنَ الأدلَّةِ أيضاً على استحالةِ التعدُّدِ في الصفاتِ المُتعلقة : أنَّها لو تعددَتْ لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ تتعدد بتعدُّدِ المتعلقاتِ ؛ بحيثُ يكون لكلِّ مُتعلَّقٍ صفةٌ تخصُّهُ ، أو لا يتعدَّد بتعددِها
والأوَّلُ مستحيل ؛ لما يلزمُ عليهِ مِنْ دخول ما لا نهاية لعددِهِ في الوجودِ ؛ لما عرفْتَ أنَّ المتعلَّقاتِ لا نهاية لها ، فإِذا قُدِّرَ أَنَّهُ وُجِدَ مِنَ الصفاتِ ما يماثلها في العددِ . . لزم المحذورُ المذكور .
والثاني مستحيل ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يفي ما يتناهى عدده بما لا يتناهى عدده ؛ لوجوب الاعتدال في قسم المتعلقاتِ على عددِ المتعلّق بها ، إذ الاختلافُ في قَسْمِ ذلكَ معَ استواء الصفاتِ يستلزمُ الافتقار إلى المخصص ، ويلزمُ عليهِ الحدوث (۱) ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
(۱) كلُّ ما وُجد في الخارج فهو متناه ، وعليه : يلزم تناهي الصفات المتعددة المفروضة ، وذاك باطل ؛ لاحتياج خصوص العدد المرجح ومخصص ، والقديم قائم بنفسه ، وإن عكسنا لزم وجود ما لا نهاية له في الخارج ، وهو مستحيل ؛ إذ كل حادث يلزمه الابتداء والانتهاء أو المدد .
And among the proofs also for the impossibility of multiplicity in the related attributes (al-ṣifāt al-mutaʿalliqa) is that if they were multiple, they would not be free from either being multiplied according to the multiplicity of objects, such that each object has a property specific to it, or not being multiplied according to their multiplicity. The first is impossible, due to what follows from it of the entry into existence of what has no end to its number; because you know that the objects have no end. So if it were supposed that of the attributes there existed what equaled them in number, the mentioned impossibility would follow. The second is impossible, because it would require that what is finite in number suffices for what is infinite in number, due to the necessity of equal distribution of objects according to the number of that which relates to them, for the difference in the division of that with the equality of the attributes necessitates need for a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ), and contingency (ḥudūth) would follow from it. And with Allah—exalted is He—is success. (1) Everything that exists in external reality is finite. And accordingly, the finitude of the supposed multiple attributes would follow, and that is invalid due to the need for a specific number, a preponderator, and a specifier, while the eternal is self-subsistent. And if we reverse it, the existence of what has no end in external reality would follow, and that is impossible because every contingent thing necessarily has a beginning and an end or durations.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the impossibility of infinite multiplicity or finite covering infinite. It analyzes the hypothetical multiplicity of related attributes into two possibilities: either the attributes multiply according to the number of objects (each object gets its own attribute) or they do not. The first leads to an infinite number of attributes (since objects are infinite), which is impossible as it would require an infinite number of attributes existing. The second leads to a finite number of attributes covering infinite objects, which requires equal distribution only if the attributes are similar; any difference would require a specifier, leading to contingency. Thus both possibilities are impossible, proving that multiplicity cannot occur.

Misconception: One might think that finite attributes could cover infinite objects by each attribute covering many objects equally without a specifier.
Correction: The argument shows that without a specifier, the distribution must be equal, but any difference in how the finite attributes relate to infinite objects would require a specifier, leading to contingency. Thus the second horn is also impossible.

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity of related attributes is impossible because both infinite multiplicity and finite multiplicity covering infinite objects lead to contradictions.

Proof of Impossibility of Multiplicity in Attributes
Sanusi proves impossibility of multiple related attributes via two impossible possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that multiplicity in God's related attributes is impossible, as it leads to either an infinite regress or a need for a particulariser.

Core Idea
Multiple attributes for God's objects leads to logical impossibility either way.

Sanusi now tackles a key objection: if God relates to many objects, why not have many attributes? He presents a rigorous proof showing that any attempt to multiply attributes leads to absurd consequences. This passage sharpens our understanding of divine simplicity and the nature of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the argument. Notice the conditional structure: 'if multiple, then either... or...'.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Sanusi divides the scenario into two mutually exclusive options: either attributes multiply with objects (infinite) or they do not (finite). Write them down.
The first leads to infinite attributes, the second to finite attributes relating to infinite objects.
3
Understand the refutation of the first possibility
If attributes are as many as objects, and objects are infinite, then attributes would be infinite. But an actual infinite cannot exist in reality. Hence impossible.
This is based on the principle that the external world cannot contain an actual infinite.
4
Understand the refutation of the second possibility
If attributes are finite while objects are infinite, then distribution would be uneven unless a particulariser chooses which attribute relates to which object. That particulariser implies temporality, which is impossible for God.
This argument uses the need for a mukhassis (particulariser) to avoid arbitrary assignment.
5
Reflect on the footnote
The footnote strengthens the proof: any supposed multiple attributes would be finite (since everything in existence is finite), and their specific number needs a cause, contradicting divine self-subsistence.
It reinforces that the only way out would be infinite attributes, which is also impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِمَّا ألا يُحصلا للذاتِ ذلكَ اللازم ، فيلزم أن يكونا وُجِدا بدونِ لازمهما الذي يستحيلُ أنْ يُوجَدا عاريين عنه ، وذلك كلُّهُ لا يُعقَلُ ومِنَ الأدلَّةِ أيضاً على استحالةِ التعدُّدِ في الصفاتِ المُتعلقة : أنَّها لو تعددَتْ لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ تتعدد بتعدُّدِ المتعلقاتِ ؛ بحيثُ يكون لكلِّ مُتعلَّقٍ صفةٌ تخصُّهُ ، أو لا يتعدَّد بتعددِها • والأوَّلُ مستحيل ؛ لما يلزمُ عليهِ مِنْ دخول ما لا نهاية لعددِهِ في الوجودِ ؛ لما عرفْتَ أنَّ المتعلَّقاتِ لا نهاية لها ، فإِذا قُدِّرَ أَنَّهُ وُجِدَ مِنَ الصفاتِ ما يماثلها في العددِ . . لزم المحذورُ المذكور . والثاني مستحيل ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يفي ما يتناهى عدده بما لا يتناهى عدده ؛ لوجوب الاعتدال في قسم المتعلقاتِ على عددِ المتعلّق بها ، إذ الاختلافُ في قَسْمِ ذلكَ معَ استواء الصفاتِ يستلزمُ الافتقار إلى المخصص ، ويلزمُ عليهِ الحدوث (١) ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق . (١) كلُّ ما وُجد في الخارج فهو متناه ، وعليه : يلزم تناهي الصفات المتعددة المفروضة ، وذاك باطل ؛ لاحتياج خصوص العدد المرجح ومخصص ، والقديم قائم بنفسه ، وإن عكسنا لزم وجود ما لا نهاية له في الخارج ، وهو مستحيل ؛ إذ كل حادث يلزمه الابتداء والانتهاء أو المدد .
Or that that necessary [consequence] is not attained for the essence, so it follows that they (the two attributes) exist without their necessary concomitant which it is impossible that they exist stripped of, and all that is unintelligible. And among the proofs also for the impossibility of multiplicity in related attributes is that if they were multiple, they would not be free from one of two cases: either they become multiple by the multiplicity of the objects of relation, such that each object has a specific attribute, or they do not become multiple by their multiplicity. The first is impossible because of what follows from it of the entry of an infinite number into existence; since you know that the objects of relation are infinite, if it is supposed that there exist attributes equal in number, the mentioned absurdity follows. The second is impossible because it would entail that that which is finite in number suffices for that which is infinite in number, due to the necessity of proportionality in the division of objects of relation according to the number of that to which they relate, for the difference in that division with the equality of the attributes necessitates the need for a particulariser, and temporality follows from that. And by Allah Almighty is success. (1) Everything that exists in the external world is finite, and accordingly: the finitude of the supposed multiple attributes follows, and that is false because of needing a particular number and particulariser, and the eternal is self-subsisting, and if we reverse it, the existence of the infinite in the external world follows, and that is impossible because every temporal thing necessitates beginning and ending or durations.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the impossibility of multiplicity in the attributes related to God's objects of relation. The author begins by referring to a previous argument about necessary concomitants, then states the second proof. He divides the scenario of multiple attributes into two possibilities: either there are as many attributes as there are objects of relation (infinite), or the number of attributes is finite while objects are infinite. Both are impossible. The first leads to an infinite number of attributes existing, which is impossible because existence cannot contain an actual infinite. The second leads to a finite number of attributes covering an infinite number of objects, which requires an unequal distribution unless a particulariser is assumed, but that implies temporality in God. The footnote clarifies that everything in external existence is finite, so the supposed multiple attributes would be finite, and their specific number would need a particulariser, which is impossible for the eternal, self-subsisting being. Conversely, if we assume infinite attributes, that is also impossible because every temporal thing has a beginning and end. Thus multiplicity is refuted.

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity in God's related attributes is impossible because both logical possibilities—infinite attributes or finite attributes with a particulariser—lead to absurdities.

The Argument Against God Being Composite
Sanusi presents a two-part argument against divine composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if God were composed of parts, it would lead to polytheism or temporal origination, neither of which is possible for God.

Core Idea
Divine composition leads to impossible consequences, so God must be non-composite.

Sanusi begins by stating that God must be one in essence, meaning not composed of parts. He then presents a powerful argument: if God were composite, either each part would be divine (leading to multiple gods) or only some parts would be divine (requiring a specifier, implying temporal origination). Both outcomes are impossible, so God cannot be composite.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from 'ويلزم' to 'الصفات' once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure 'لو تركيب... لم يخل' (if He were composed... it would not be free from).
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two possibilities if God were composite. List them and understand their consequences.
The two options are exhaustive: either every part has divine attributes, or only some do.
3
Analyze the first possibility
If every part had divine attributes, each part would be a god, leading to polytheism. This is impossible for the One God.
4
Analyze the second possibility
If only some parts had divinity, those parts would need a specifier, which implies temporality (ḥudūth). This is also impossible for the eternal God.
The reasoning relies on the principle that if all parts are equal in capacity, a specifier is needed to give some an attribute.
5
Conclude the argument
Since both possibilities are impossible, the original assumption that God is composite is false. Thus, God is non-composite and one in essence.
This argument is a classic proof for tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي الكموم المستحيلة عنه سبحانه ] ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ تعالى واحداً في ذاتِهِ ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ غيرُ مُركَّب ، وإلا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ جسماً ، وأيضاً : فلو تركَّبَ مِنْ جزأين فأكثر لم يخلُ : إما أن يقوم بكل جزء صفات الألوهية، أو يختص قيامها بالبعض ، والأَوَّلُ يلزم منه تعدُّدُ الآلهة والثاني يلزم منه الحدوث ؛ للاحتياج إلى المخصص بعضها بصفاتِ الأُلوهِيَّةِ ؛ لاستواء جميعها في قبولِ تلك الصفاتِ وليس معنى نفي التركيب في الذاتِ العليَّةِ أنَّها جزء لا يتجزأ ، وإلا لزمَ أنْ تكون جوهراً فرداً ، وقد سبق استحالةُ الجرْميَّةِ عليهِ مطلقاً (۱) ، وإنَّما المقصود : أنَّ الذات العليَّةَ لا تقبلُ صِغَراً ولا كِبَراً ؛ لأنَّهما مِنْ عوارض الأجرام ، وهو تعالى يستحيل أن يكون جزماً . يعني : أَنَّهُ يجبُ عقلاً وشرعاً ثبوتُ الوَحدانيَّةِ لهُ تعالى في ذاتِهِ وفي صفاته وفي أفعاله (۱) تقدم ( ص ٢٤٢ ) .
[Negation of impossible quantities concerning Him, exalted be He] And it is necessary that He, exalted be He, be one in His essence; meaning: that He is not composite, otherwise it would be necessary that He be a body. Also: if He were composed of two or more parts, it would not be free from either: that the attributes of divinity subsist in every part, or their subsistence is specific to some parts. The first necessitates multiplicity of gods, and the second necessitates temporal origination (ḥudūth); due to the need for a specifier of some of them with the attributes of divinity, because all of them are equal in accepting those attributes. And the meaning of negation of composition (nafy al-tarkīb) in the supreme essence is not that it is an indivisible part, otherwise it would be necessary that it be an individual atom (jawhar fard), and the impossibility of corporeality (jirmiyya) regarding Him has already been established absolutely. Rather, the intended meaning is that the supreme essence does not accept smallness or largeness; because they are accidents of bodies, and He, exalted be He, is impossible to be a body. That is: it is rationally and legally necessary to affirm unity (waḥdāniyya) for Him, exalted be He, in His essence, His attributes, and His actions. (1) See (p. 242).
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument establishing that God is one in His essence, meaning not composed of parts. The author begins by stating the requirement that God be one in essence, meaning non-composite; otherwise He would be a body. He then presents an argument: if God were composed of two or more parts, two possibilities arise. First, each part possesses divine attributes, leading to multiple gods. Second, only some parts possess them, which requires a specifier to choose which parts get divinity, implying temporal origination (since the parts are equal in capacity). Both consequences are impossible. The author then clarifies that negating composition does not mean God is an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which would still be a body; rather, it means the divine essence is not subject to size or dimension, as those are accidents of bodies. He concludes that tawḥīd (divine unity) is rationally and legally obligatory in essence, attributes, and actions. The footnote refers to an earlier discussion on the impossibility of corporeality.

A) Each part would be a god.
B) God would be a body.
C) It would necessitate temporal origination.
D) It would prove God's unity.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be composite because composition leads to polytheism or temporality, both impossible for the eternal One.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that any composition leads to absurdity. Reflect on your own life: do you try to compartmentalize God's attributes, treating them as separate parts? How can you train your heart to see God's attributes as unified in His essence? What practical step will you take today to deepen your tawheed?
?
The need for a specifier shows that if God were composite, He would be dependent on a cause. Do you ever act as if God is dependent on your actions or that He 'needs' your worship? Ponder the meaning of God's absolute independence (al-Ṣamad). How does this lesson remind you that your worship is for your own benefit, not His? Write a resolution to worship with consciousness of His perfection.

Understanding Divine Unity Beyond Composition
Sanusi clarifies the correct understanding of negation of composition and affirms comprehensive tawḥīd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that negating composition in God does not mean He is an indivisible atom, but rather that He is not subject to size or dimension, and that unity (tawḥīd) must be affirmed in essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
God's non-composition is not atomism; He is free from all physical attributes.

After proving that God is not composite, Sanusi clarifies a potential misunderstanding: negating composition does not mean God is an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), as that would still imply being a body. Instead, it means the divine essence is not subject to smallness or largeness—accidents of bodies. He then concludes that tawḥīd (divine unity) is rationally and legally obligatory in essence, attributes, and actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from 'وليس' to 'أفعاله' to grasp the clarification and conclusion.
Pay attention to the contrast 'ليس... بل' (not... but rather).
2
Understand the misconception
The author warns against thinking that non-composite means an indivisible atom, which would still be a body. Understand why this is wrong.
An indivisible atom (jawhar fard) is a physical concept from kalām cosmology.
3
Grasp the positive meaning
The true meaning is that God's essence does not accept smallness or largeness, as these are attributes of bodies.
This negates all physical dimensions from God.
4
Conclude with the scope of unity
The passage ends by affirming tawḥīd in three aspects: essence, attributes, and actions. This is the comprehensive tawḥīd.
This tripartite division is a standard formulation.

Source Text

[Negation of impossible quantities concerning Him, exalted be He] And it is necessary that He, exalted be He, be one in His essence; meaning: that He is not composite, otherwise it would be necessary that He be a body. Also: if He were composed of two or more parts, it would not be free from either: that the attributes of divinity subsist in every part, or their subsistence is specific to some parts. The first necessitates multiplicity of gods, and the second necessitates temporal origination (ḥudūth); due to the need for a specifier of some of them with the attributes of divinity, because all of them are equal in accepting those attributes. And the meaning of negation of composition (nafy al-tarkīb) in the supreme essence is not that it is an indivisible part, otherwise it would be necessary that it be an individual atom (jawhar fard), and the impossibility of corporeality (jirmiyya) regarding Him has already been established absolutely. Rather, the intended meaning is that the supreme essence does not accept smallness or largeness; because they are accidents of bodies, and He, exalted be He, is impossible to be a body. That is: it is rationally and legally necessary to affirm unity (waḥdāniyya) for Him, exalted be He, in His essence, His attributes, and His actions. (1) See (p. 242).
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument establishing that God is one in His essence, meaning not composed of parts. The author begins by stating the requirement that God be one in essence, meaning non-composite; otherwise He would be a body. He then presents an argument: if God were composed of two or more parts, two possibilities arise. First, each part possesses divine attributes, leading to multiple gods. Second, only some parts possess them, which requires a specifier to choose which parts get divinity, implying temporal origination (since the parts are equal in capacity). Both consequences are impossible. The author then clarifies that negating composition does not mean God is an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which would still be a body; rather, it means the divine essence is not subject to size or dimension, as those are accidents of bodies. He concludes that tawḥīd (divine unity) is rationally and legally obligatory in essence, attributes, and actions. The footnote refers to an earlier discussion on the impossibility of corporeality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that when we say God is not composite, it means He is an indivisible atom (jawhar fard).
Correction: The atom is still a physical entity; God is not a body at all. Non-composition means He is free from all quantity and dimension.

Checkpoint
What does 'negation of composition' (nafy al-tarkīb) mean according to the passage?
A) God is an indivisible atom.
B) God does not accept smallness or largeness.
C) God is composed of spiritual parts.
D) God is a body without dimensions.

Key Takeaway
God's non-composition means He is beyond all physical dimensions, and tawḥīd encompasses His essence, attributes, and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously picture God as a 'small' or 'simple' entity, like an atom, without realizing it? How does this lesson challenge that mental image, and what does it mean for your reverence of Him?
?
If tawḥīd is not just about believing in one God but also affirming His oneness in your actions, where in your life do you act as if other forces truly cause outcomes? What would it look like to sincerely attribute everything to Him starting today?
?
When you recite 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh', do you limit it to essence only, or do you also affirm His unique attributes and His sole control over all events? What practical step can you take today to worship Him as the One in all three aspects?
